           <li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0">
            <i>
             <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aublysodon_cristatus" title="Aublysodon cristatus">
              Aublysodon cristatus
             </a>
            </i>
            ?
            <br/>
            <small>
             Marsh, 1892
            </small>
           </li>
           <li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0">
            <i>

            <br/>
            <small>

            </small>
           </li>

           </li>

            </small>
           </li>

           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       <b>
        Tyrannosaurus
       </b>
      </i>
      (
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
           t
          </span>
          <span title="// 'e' in 'roses'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="// secondary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
           r
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'a' in 'bad'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'n' in 'no'">
           n
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
           s
          </span>
          <span title="/r/ 'or' in 'born'">
           r
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
           s
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      or
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
           t
          </span>
          <span title="/a/ long 'i' in 'tide'">
           a
          </span>
          <span title="// secondary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
           r
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'a' in 'bad'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'n' in 'no'">
           n
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
           s
          </span>
          <span title="/r/ 'or' in 'born'">
           r
          </span>
          <span title="// 'a' in 'about'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
           s
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      ("tyrant lizard", from the
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
       Ancient Greek
      </a>
      <i>
       tyrannos
      </i>
      (), "tyrant", and
      <i>
       sauros
      </i>
      (), "lizard"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      )) is a
      <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">
       genus
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coelurosaur" title="Coelurosaur">
       coelurosaurian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">
       theropod
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">
       dinosaur
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">
       species
      </a>
      <i>
       <b>
        Tyrannosaurus rex
       </b>
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       rex
      </i>
      meaning "king" in
      <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
       Latin
      </a>
      ), commonly abbreviated to
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      , is one of the most well-represented of the large theropods.
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      lived throughout what is now western North America, which then was an island continent named
      <a href="/wiki/Laramidia" title="Laramidia">
       Laramidia
      </a>
      .
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      had a much wider range than other
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">
       tyrannosaurids
      </a>
      . Fossils are found in a variety of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geologic_formation" title="Geologic formation">
       rock formations
      </a>
      dating to the
      <a href="/wiki/Maastrichtian" title="Maastrichtian">
       Maastrichtian
      </a>
      age of the upper
      <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">
       Cretaceous
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Period_(geology)" title="Period (geology)">
       Period
      </a>
      , 68 to 66
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">
       million years ago
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hellcreekage_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hellcreekage-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was the last known member of the
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">
       tyrannosaurids
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and among the last non-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aves" title="Aves">
       avian
      </a>
      dinosaurs, to exist before the
      <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="CretaceousPaleogene extinction event">
       CretaceousPaleogene extinction event
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Like other tyrannosaurids,
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biped" title="Biped">
       bipedal
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">
       carnivore
      </a>
      with a massive skull balanced by a long, heavy tail. Relative to its large and powerful hind limbs,
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      fore limbs were short but unusually powerful for their size and had two clawed digits. Although other theropods rivaled or exceeded
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_size" title="Dinosaur size">
       size
      </a>
      , it was the largest known tyrannosaurid and one of the largest known land predators. In fact, the most complete specimen measures up to 12.3m (40ft) in length,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinsonet.al.2011_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hutchinsonet.al.2011-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      up to 4 metres (13ft) tall at the hips,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SueFMNH_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SueFMNH-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and up to 6.8 metric tons (7.5 short tons) in weight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ericksonetal2004_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ericksonetal2004-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By far the largest carnivore in its environment,
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      may have been an
      <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">
       apex predator
      </a>
      , preying upon
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadrosaur" title="Hadrosaur">
       hadrosaurs
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">
       ceratopsians
      </a>
      , and possibly sauropods,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SwitekChompedSauropod_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SwitekChompedSauropod-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although some experts have suggested the dinosaur was primarily a
      <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">
       scavenger
      </a>
      . The debate about whether
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was an apex predator or scavenger was among the longest ongoing feud in
      <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">
       paleontology
      </a>
      ; however, most scientists now agree that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      was an opportunistic carnivore, acting as both a predator and a scavenger.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonTrexPredatorMediaHype_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonTrexPredatorMediaHype-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is estimated to be capable of exerting one of the largest bite forces among all terrestrial animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MM03_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MM03-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bs_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bs-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      More than 50specimens of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      have been identified, some of which are nearly complete skeletons.
      <a href="/wiki/Soft_tissue" title="Soft tissue">
       Soft tissue
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
       proteins
      </a>
      have been reported in at least one of these specimens. The abundance of fossil material has allowed significant research into many aspects of its biology, including its life history and
      <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">
       biomechanics
      </a>
      . The feeding habits,
      <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">
       physiology
      </a>
      and potential speed of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      are a few subjects of debate. Its
      <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">
       taxonomy
      </a>
      is also controversial, as some scientists consider
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tarbosaurus" title="Tarbosaurus">
        Tarbosaurus bataar
       </a>
      </i>
      from Asia to be a second
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      species while others maintain
      <i>
       Tarbosaurus
      </i>
      is a separate genus. Several other genera of North American tyrannosaurids have also been
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synonym_(biology)" title="Synonym (biology)">
       synonymized
      </a>
      with
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">
       Description
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Largesttheropods.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="1339" height="71" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Largesttheropods.png/220px-Largesttheropods.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Largesttheropods.png/330px-Largesttheropods.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Largesttheropods.png/440px-Largesttheropods.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Largesttheropods.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Size (in green) compared with selected giant theropods
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      was one of the largest land carnivores of all time; the largest complete specimen, located at the
      <a href="/wiki/Field_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Field Museum of Natural History">
       Field Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      and known colloquially as FMNH PR2081 and nicknamed "
      <a href="/wiki/Sue_(dinosaur)" title="Sue (dinosaur)">
       Sue
      </a>
      ", measured 12.3 metres (40ft) long,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinsonet.al.2011_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hutchinsonet.al.2011-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and was 4 metres (13ft) tall at the hips.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SueFMNH_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SueFMNH-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mass estimates have varied widely over the years, from more than 7.2 metric tons (7.9 short tons),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-henderson1999_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-henderson1999-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to less than 4.5 metric tons (5.0 short tons),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-andersonetal1985_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-andersonetal1985-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1986_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bakker1986-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
         <span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2010)">
          pageneeded
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      with most modern estimates ranging between 5.4 metric tons (6.0 short tons) and 6.8 metric tons (7.5 short tons).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ericksonetal2004_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ericksonetal2004-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farlowetal1995_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-farlowetal1995-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seebacher2001_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seebacher2001-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christiansenfarina2004_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-christiansenfarina2004-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One study in 2011 found that the maximum weight of Sue, the largest
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , was between 9.5 and 18.5 metric tons (9.318.2 long tons; 10.520.4 short tons), though the authors stated that their upper and lower estimates were based on models with wide error bars and that they "consider [them] to be too skinny, too fat, or too disproportionate".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinsonet.al.2011_4-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hutchinsonet.al.2011-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Packard
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (2009) tested dinosaur mass estimation procedures on elephants and concluded that those of dinosaurs are flawed and produce over-estimations; thus, the weight of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      could have been much less than previously thought.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other estimations have concluded that the largest known
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      specimens had masses approaching
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HartmanMassEstimate_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HartmanMassEstimate-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or exceeding 9 tonnes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinsonet.al.2011_4-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hutchinsonet.al.2011-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TH07-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_rex_mmartyniuk.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="1975" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tyrannosaurus_rex_mmartyniuk.png/220px-Tyrannosaurus_rex_mmartyniuk.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tyrannosaurus_rex_mmartyniuk.png/330px-Tyrannosaurus_rex_mmartyniuk.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tyrannosaurus_rex_mmartyniuk.png/440px-Tyrannosaurus_rex_mmartyniuk.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_rex_mmartyniuk.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Life restoration of an adult
        <i>
         T.rex
        </i>
        with feathers (a trait that can be inferred by
        <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_bracketing" title="Phylogenetic bracketing">
         phylogenetic bracketing
        </a>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The neck of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      formed a natural S-shaped curve like that of other theropods, but was short and muscular to support the massive head. The forelimbs had only two clawed fingers,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brochu2003_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-brochu2003-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      along with an additional small
      <a href="/wiki/Metacarpus" title="Metacarpus">
       metacarpal
      </a>
      representing the remnant of a third digit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CLKC08_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CLKC08-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast the hind limbs were among the longest in proportion to body size of any theropod. The tail was heavy and long, sometimes containing over forty
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">
       vertebrae
      </a>
      , in order to balance the massive head and torso. To compensate for the immense bulk of the animal, many bones throughout the skeleton were hollow, reducing its weight without significant loss of strength.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brochu2003_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-brochu2003-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannoskull.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3058" data-file-width="4095" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Tyrannoskull.jpg/220px-Tyrannoskull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Tyrannoskull.jpg/330px-Tyrannoskull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Tyrannoskull.jpg/440px-Tyrannoskull.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannoskull.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Profile view of a skull (AMNH 5027)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The largest known
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      skulls measure up to 1.5 metres (5ft) in length.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Large
      <a href="/wiki/Fenestra_(anatomy)" title="Fenestra (anatomy)">
       fenestrae
      </a>
      (openings) in the skull reduced weight and provided areas for muscle attachment, as in all carnivorous theropods. But in other respects
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      's skull was significantly different from those of large non-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrannosauroid" title="Tyrannosauroid">
       tyrannosauroid
      </a>
      theropods. It was extremely wide at the rear but had a narrow snout, allowing unusually good
      <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">
       binocular vision
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevens2006Binocular_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stevens2006Binocular-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaffe_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jaffe-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The skull bones were massive and the
      <a href="/wiki/Nasal_bone" title="Nasal bone">
       nasals
      </a>
      and some other bones were fused, preventing movement between them; but many were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pneumatized_bones" title="Pneumatized bones">
       pneumatized
      </a>
      (contained a "honeycomb" of tiny air spaces) which may have made the bones more flexible as well as lighter. These and other skull-strengthening features are part of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurid" title="Tyrannosaurid">
       tyrannosaurid
      </a>
      trend towards an increasingly powerful bite, which easily surpassed that of all non-tyrannosaurids.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MM03_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MM03-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SnivelyHendersonPhillips2006FusedVaultedNasals_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SnivelyHendersonPhillips2006FusedVaultedNasals-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GEetal96_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GEetal96-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The tip of the upper jaw was U-shaped (most non-tyrannosauroid carnivores had V-shaped upper jaws), which increased the amount of tissue and bone a tyrannosaur could rip out with one bite, although it also increased the stresses on the front teeth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtz1994_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-holtz1994-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paul1988_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-paul1988-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The teeth of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      displayed marked
      <a href="/wiki/Heterodont" title="Heterodont">
       heterodonty
      </a>
      (differences in shape).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brochu2003_21-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-brochu2003-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2005HeterodontyTRex_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith2005HeterodontyTRex-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Premaxilla" title="Premaxilla">
       premaxillary
      </a>
      teeth at the front of the upper jaw were closely packed, D-shaped in cross-section, had reinforcing ridges on the rear surface, were
      <a href="/wiki/Incisor" title="Incisor">
       incisiform
      </a>
      (their tips were chisel-like blades) and curved backwards. The D-shaped cross-section, reinforcing ridges and backwards curve reduced the risk that the teeth would snap when
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      bit and pulled. The remaining teeth were robust, like "lethal bananas" rather than daggers; more widely spaced and also had reinforcing ridges.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Scientist1998DinosaurDetectives_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist1998DinosaurDetectives-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Those in the upper jaw were larger than those in all but the rear of the lower jaw. The largest found so far is estimated to have been 30 centimetres (12in) long including the root when the animal was alive, making it the largest tooth of any carnivorous dinosaur yet found.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SueFMNH_5-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-SueFMNH-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Skin_and_feathers">
       Skin and feathers
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaur" title="Feathered dinosaur">
       Feathered dinosaur
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Feathered_Tyrannosaurus_model.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Feathered_Tyrannosaurus_model.jpg/220px-Feathered_Tyrannosaurus_model.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Feathered_Tyrannosaurus_model.jpg/330px-Feathered_Tyrannosaurus_model.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Feathered_Tyrannosaurus_model.jpg/440px-Feathered_Tyrannosaurus_model.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Feathered_Tyrannosaurus_model.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Full size model in Poland, depicting
        <i>
         Tyrannosaurus
        </i>
        with both feathers and scales
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      While there is no direct evidence for
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      having had feathers, many scientists now consider it likely that
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      had feathers on at least parts of its body,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hone_guardian2012_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hone_guardian2012-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      due to their presence in related species of similar size. Mark Norell of the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">
       American Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      summarized the balance of evidence by stating that: "we have as much evidence that
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      was feathered, at least during some stage of its life, as we do that
      <a href="/wiki/Australopithecine" title="Australopithecine">
       australopithecines
      </a>
      like
      <a href="/wiki/Lucy_(Australopithecus)" title="Lucy (Australopithecus)">
       Lucy
      </a>
      had hair."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keim_wired2012_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-keim_wired2012-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The first evidence for feathers in tyrannosauroids came from the small species
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dilong_paradoxus" title="Dilong paradoxus">
        Dilong paradoxus
       </a>
      </i>
      , found in the
      <a href="/wiki/Yixian_Formation" title="Yixian Formation">
       Yixian Formation
      </a>
      of China, and reported in the journal
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">
        Nature
       </a>
      </i>
      in 2004. As with many other theropods discovered in the Yixian, the fossil skeleton was preserved with a coat of filamentous structures which are commonly recognized as the precursors of
      <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">
       feathers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xuetal2004_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-xuetal2004-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because all known skin impressions from larger tyrannosauroids known at the time showed evidence of scales, the researchers who studied
      <i>
       Dilong
      </i>
      speculated that feathers may correlate negatively with body sizethat juveniles may have been feathered, then shed the feathers and expressed only scales as the animal became larger and no longer needed insulation to stay warm.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xuetal2004_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-xuetal2004-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, subsequent discoveries showed that even some gigantic tyrannosauroids had feathers covering much of their bodies, casting doubt on the hypothesis that they were a size-related feature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yutyrannus_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-yutyrannus-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      While skin impressions from a
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      specimen nicknamed "Wyrex" (BHI 6230) discovered in Montana in 2002,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2008_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-larson2008-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as some other giant tyrannosauroid specimens, show at least small patches of mosaic scales,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP08_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GSP08-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      others, such as
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yutyrannus_huali" title="Yutyrannus huali">
        Yutyrannus huali
       </a>
      </i>
      (which was up to 9 metres (30ft) long and weighed about 1,400 kilograms (3,100lb)), preserve feathers on various sections of the body, strongly suggesting that its whole body was covered in feathers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yutyrannus_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-yutyrannus-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is possible that the extent and nature of feather covering in tyrannosauroids may have changed over time in response to body size, a warmer climate, or other factors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yutyrannus_35-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-yutyrannus-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
       Classification
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      is the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_type" title="Biological type">
       type
      </a>
      genus of the superfamily
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauroidea" title="Tyrannosauroidea">
       Tyrannosauroidea
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">
       family
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">
       Tyrannosauridae
      </a>
      , and the subfamily Tyrannosaurinae; in other words it is the standard by which paleontologists decide whether to include other species in the same group. Other members of the tyrannosaurine subfamily include the North American
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Daspletosaurus" title="Daspletosaurus">
        Daspletosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">
       Asian
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tarbosaurus" title="Tarbosaurus">
        Tarbosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-currieetal2003_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-currieetal2003-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtz2004_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-holtz2004-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      both of which have occasionally been synonymized with
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paul1988_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-paul1988-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
         <span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2010)">
          pageneeded
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Tyrannosaurids were once commonly thought to be descendants of earlier large theropods such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinosauroidea" title="Spinosauroidea">
       megalosaurs
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Carnosauria" title="Carnosauria">
       carnosaurs
      </a>
      , although more recently they were reclassified with the generally smaller
      <a href="/wiki/Coelurosauria" title="Coelurosauria">
       coelurosaurs
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtz1994_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-holtz1994-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tarbosaurus_and_Tyrannosaurus.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3130" data-file-width="5927" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Tarbosaurus_and_Tyrannosaurus.jpg/220px-Tarbosaurus_and_Tyrannosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Tarbosaurus_and_Tyrannosaurus.jpg/330px-Tarbosaurus_and_Tyrannosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Tarbosaurus_and_Tyrannosaurus.jpg/440px-Tarbosaurus_and_Tyrannosaurus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tarbosaurus_and_Tyrannosaurus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Diagram showing the differences between a generalised
        <i>
         Tarbosaurus
        </i>
        (A) and
        <i>
         Tyrannosaurus
        </i>
        (B) skull
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nanotyrannus_lancensis_skull.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1589" data-file-width="2699" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nanotyrannus_lancensis_skull.jpg/220px-Nanotyrannus_lancensis_skull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nanotyrannus_lancensis_skull.jpg/330px-Nanotyrannus_lancensis_skull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nanotyrannus_lancensis_skull.jpg/440px-Nanotyrannus_lancensis_skull.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nanotyrannus_lancensis_skull.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Nanotyrannus lancensis
        </i>
        holotype, possibly a juvenile
        <i>
         Tyrannosaurus
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1955, Soviet
      <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">
       paleontologist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Evgeny_Maleev" title="Evgeny Maleev">
       Evgeny Maleev
      </a>
      named a new species,
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus bataar
      </i>
      , from
      <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">
       Mongolia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maleev1955_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-maleev1955-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 1965, this species had been renamed
      <i>
       Tarbosaurus bataar
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rozhdestvensky1965_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rozhdestvensky1965-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite the renaming, many
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">
       phylogenetic
      </a>
      analyses have found
      <i>
       Tarbosaurus bataar
      </i>
      to be the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sister_taxon" title="Sister taxon">
       sister taxon
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtz2004_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-holtz2004-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and it has often been considered an Asian species of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtz1994_28-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-holtz1994-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter1992_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carpenter1992-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carretal2005_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carretal2005-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A recent redescription of the skull of
      <i>
       Tarbosaurus bataar
      </i>
      has shown that it was much narrower than that of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      and that during a bite, the distribution of stress in the skull would have been very different, closer to that of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Alioramus" title="Alioramus">
        Alioramus
       </a>
      </i>
      , another Asian tyrannosaur.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hurumsabath2003_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hurumsabath2003-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A related
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cladistic" title="Cladistic">
       cladistic
      </a>
      analysis found that
      <i>
       Alioramus
      </i>
      , not
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , was the sister taxon of
      <i>
       Tarbosaurus
      </i>
      , which, if true, would suggest that
      <i>
       Tarbosaurus
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      should remain separate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-currieetal2003_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-currieetal2003-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other tyrannosaurid fossils found in the same formations as
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      were originally classified as separate taxa, including
      <i>
       Aublysodon
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Albertosaurus megagracilis
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paul1988_29-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-paul1988-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the latter being named
      <i>
       Dinotyrannus megagracilis
      </i>
      in 1995.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olshevsky1995_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Olshevsky1995-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, these fossils are now universally considered to belong to juvenile
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carrwilliamson2004_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carrwilliamson2004-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A small but nearly complete skull from Montana, 60 centimetres (2.0ft) long, may be an exception. This skull was originally classified as a species of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Gorgosaurus" title="Gorgosaurus">
        Gorgosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       G. lancensis
      </i>
      ) by
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Gilmore" title="Charles W. Gilmore">
       Charles W. Gilmore
      </a>
      in 1946,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gilmore1946_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gilmore1946-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but was later referred to a new genus,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nanotyrannus" title="Nanotyrannus">
        Nanotyrannus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakkeretal1988_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bakkeretal1988-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Opinions remain divided on the validity of
      <i>
       N. lancensis
      </i>
      . Many paleontologists consider the skull to belong to a juvenile
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carr1999_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carr1999-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are minor differences between the two species, including the higher number of teeth in
      <i>
       N. lancensis
      </i>
      , which lead some scientists to recommend keeping the two genera separate until further research or discoveries clarify the situation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtz2004_39-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-holtz2004-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-currie2003_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-currie2003-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Below is the cladogram of Tyrannosauridae based on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_analysis" title="Phylogenetic analysis">
       phylogenetic analysis
      </a>
      conducted by Loewen
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      in 2013.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loewen13_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Loewen13-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

                            </td>
                           </tr>
                           <tr>
                            <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">

                            </td>
                            <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">

                              <tr>
                               <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">

                                <p>
                                 <i>
                                  <b>
                                   Tyrannosaurus rex
                                  </b>
                                 </i>
                                 <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_AMNH_5027_(white_background).jpg">
                                  <img alt="Tyrannosaurus AMNH 5027 (white background).jpg" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="38" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Tyrannosaurus_AMNH_5027_%28white_background%29.jpg/50px-Tyrannosaurus_AMNH_5027_%28white_background%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Tyrannosaurus_AMNH_5027_%28white_background%29.jpg/75px-Tyrannosaurus_AMNH_5027_%28white_background%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Tyrannosaurus_AMNH_5027_%28white_background%29.jpg/100px-Tyrannosaurus_AMNH_5027_%28white_background%29.jpg 2x" width="50"/>
                                 </a>
                                </p>
                               </td>
                              </tr>
                              <tr>
                               <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                                <br/>
                               </td>
                              </tr>
                              <tr>
                               <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                                <br/>
                               </td>
                               <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                                <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="">
                                 <tr>
                                  <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                                   <br/>
                                  </td>
                                  <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                                   <p>
                                    <i>
                                     <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarbosaurus_bataar" title="Tarbosaurus bataar">
                                      Tarbosaurus bataar
                                     </a>
                                    </i>
                                    <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yamanashigakuin_elementary_school_Tarbosaurus_white_background.JPG">
                                     <img alt="Yamanashigakuin elementary school Tarbosaurus white background.JPG" data-file-height="2022" data-file-width="3572" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Yamanashigakuin_elementary_school_Tarbosaurus_white_background.JPG/50px-Yamanashigakuin_elementary_school_Tarbosaurus_white_background.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Yamanashigakuin_elementary_school_Tarbosaurus_white_background.JPG/75px-Yamanashigakuin_elementary_school_Tarbosaurus_white_background.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Yamanashigakuin_elementary_school_Tarbosaurus_white_background.JPG/100px-Yamanashigakuin_elementary_school_Tarbosaurus_white_background.JPG 2x" width="50"/>
                                    </a>
                                   </p>
                                  </td>
                                 </tr>
                                 <tr>
                                  <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">

                                  </td>
                                 </tr>
                                 <tr>
                                  <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                                   <br/>
                                  </td>
                                  <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                                   <p>
                                    <i>
                                     <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhuchengtyrannus_magnus" title="Zhuchengtyrannus magnus">
                                      Zhuchengtyrannus magnus
                                     </a>
                                    </i>
                                   </p>
                                  </td>
                                 </tr>
                                 <tr>
                                  <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                                   <br/>

                               </td>
                              </tr>
                              <tr>

                            </td>

                           <tr>
                            <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">

                         </td>
                        </tr>

                         <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                          <br/>
                         </td>

                      </td>
                     </tr>

                      </td>

                   </td>
                  </tr>

                   <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">

                   </td>

     </table>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Paleobiology">
       Paleobiology
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Life_history">
       Life history
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrantgraph.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="572" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tyrantgraph.png/300px-Tyrantgraph.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tyrantgraph.png/450px-Tyrantgraph.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Tyrantgraph.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrantgraph.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A graph showing the hypothesized growth curve, body mass versus age (drawn in black, with other tyrannosaurids for comparison). Based on Erickson et al. 2004
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The identification of several specimens as juvenile
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      has allowed scientists to document
      <a href="/wiki/Ontogeny" title="Ontogeny">
       ontogenetic
      </a>
      changes in the species, estimate the lifespan, and determine how quickly the animals would have grown. The smallest known individual (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_County_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Los Angeles County Museum of Natural History">
       LACM
      </a>
      28471, the "Jordan theropod") is estimated to have weighed only 30kg (66lb), while the largest, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Field_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Field Museum of Natural History">
       FMNH
      </a>
      PR2081 ("
      <a href="/wiki/Sue_(dinosaur)" title="Sue (dinosaur)">
       Sue
      </a>
      ") most likely weighed over 5,400kg (11,900lb).
      <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">
       Histologic
      </a>
      analysis of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      bones showed LACM 28471 had aged only 2 years when it died, while "Sue" was 28 years old, an age which may have been close to the maximum for the species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ericksonetal2004_6-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ericksonetal2004-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T-rex_fossil_Jane_by_Volkan_Yuksel_DSC08683g.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/T-rex_fossil_Jane_by_Volkan_Yuksel_DSC08683g.JPG/220px-T-rex_fossil_Jane_by_Volkan_Yuksel_DSC08683g.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/T-rex_fossil_Jane_by_Volkan_Yuksel_DSC08683g.JPG/330px-T-rex_fossil_Jane_by_Volkan_Yuksel_DSC08683g.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/T-rex_fossil_Jane_by_Volkan_Yuksel_DSC08683g.JPG/440px-T-rex_fossil_Jane_by_Volkan_Yuksel_DSC08683g.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T-rex_fossil_Jane_by_Volkan_Yuksel_DSC08683g.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        11-year-old juvenile ("Jane") specimen, with adult in the background,
        <a href="/wiki/Burpee_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Burpee Museum of Natural History">
         Burpee Museum of Natural History
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Histology has also allowed the age of other specimens to be determined. Growth curves can be developed when the ages of different specimens are plotted on a graph along with their mass. A
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      growth curve is S-shaped, with juveniles remaining under 1,800kg (4,000lb) until approximately 14 years of age, when body size began to increase dramatically. During this rapid growth phase, a young
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      would gain an average of 600kg (1,300lb) a year for the next four years. At 18 years of age, the curve plateaus again, indicating that growth slowed dramatically. For example, only 600kg (1,300lb) separated the 28-year-old "Sue" from a 22-year-old
      <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
       Canadian
      </a>
      specimen (
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Tyrrell_Museum_of_Palaeontology" title="Royal Tyrrell Museum of Palaeontology">
       RTMP
      </a>
      81.12.1).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ericksonetal2004_6-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-ericksonetal2004-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2004 histological study performed by different workers corroborates these results, finding that rapid growth began to slow at around 16 years of age.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hornerpadian2004_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hornerpadian2004-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another study corroborated the latter study's results but found the growth rate to be much faster, finding it to be around 1800 kilograms (4000lbs). Although these results were much higher than previous estimations, the authors noted that these results significantly lowered the great difference between its actual growth rate and the one which would be expected of an animal of its size.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinsonet.al.2011_4-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hutchinsonet.al.2011-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The sudden change in growth rate at the end of the growth spurt may indicate physical maturity, a hypothesis which is supported by the discovery of medullary tissue in the
      <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">
       femur
      </a>
      of a 16 to 20-year-old
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      from Montana (
      <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_the_Rockies" title="Museum of the Rockies">
       MOR
      </a>
      1125, also known as "B-rex"). Medullary tissue is found only in female birds during ovulation, indicating that "B-rex" was of reproductive age.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schweitzeretal2005_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-schweitzeretal2005-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Further study indicates an age of 18 for this specimen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LW08_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LW08-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other tyrannosaurids exhibit extremely similar growth curves, although with lower growth rates corresponding to their lower adult sizes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ericksonetal2006_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ericksonetal2006-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Over half of the known
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      specimens appear to have died within six years of reaching sexual maturity, a pattern which is also seen in other tyrannosaurs and in some large, long-lived birds and mammals today. These species are characterized by high infant mortality rates, followed by relatively low mortality among juveniles. Mortality increases again following sexual maturity, partly due to the stresses of reproduction. One study suggests that the rarity of juvenile
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      fossils is due in part to low juvenile mortality rates; the animals were not dying in large numbers at these ages, and so were not often fossilized. However, this rarity may also be due to the incompleteness of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">
       fossil record
      </a>
      or to the bias of fossil collectors towards larger, more spectacular specimens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ericksonetal2006_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ericksonetal2006-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a 2013 lecture, Thomas Holtz Jr. would suggest that dinosaurs "lived fast and died young" because they reproduced quickly whereas mammals have long life spans because they take longer to reproduce.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HoltzLecture2013_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HoltzLecture2013-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Gregory S. Paul also writes that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      reproduced quickly and died young, but attributes their short life spans to the dangerous lives they lived.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul2008Highrisklife_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Paul2008Highrisklife-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_dimorphism">
       Sexual dimorphism
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MUJA_04.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="638" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MUJA_04.jpg/170px-MUJA_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MUJA_04.jpg/255px-MUJA_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MUJA_04.jpg/340px-MUJA_04.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MUJA_04.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Skeleton casts mounted in a mating position,
        <a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Museum_of_Asturias" title="Jurassic Museum of Asturias">
         Jurassic Museum of Asturias
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As the number of known specimens increased, scientists began to analyze the variation between individuals and discovered what appeared to be two distinct body types, or
      <i>
       morphs
      </i>
      , similar to some other theropod species. As one of these morphs was more solidly built, it was termed the 'robust' morph while the other was termed '
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gracile" title="wikt:gracile">
       gracile
      </a>
      '. Several
      <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">
       morphological
      </a>
      differences associated with the two morphs were used to analyze
      <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">
       sexual dimorphism
      </a>
      in
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      , with the 'robust' morph usually suggested to be female. For example, the
      <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">
       pelvis
      </a>
      of several 'robust' specimens seemed to be wider, perhaps to allow the passage of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">
       eggs
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter1990_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carpenter1990-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was also thought that the 'robust' morphology correlated with a reduced
      <a href="/wiki/Chevron_(anatomy)" title="Chevron (anatomy)">
       chevron
      </a>
      on the first tail vertebra, also ostensibly to allow eggs to pass out of the
      <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_system" title="Reproductive system">
       reproductive tract
      </a>
      , as had been erroneously reported for
      <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">
       crocodiles
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson1994_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-larson1994-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In recent years, evidence for sexual dimorphism has been weakened. A 2005 study reported that previous claims of sexual dimorphism in crocodile chevron anatomy were in error, casting doubt on the existence of similar dimorphism between
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      sexes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ericksonetal2005_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ericksonetal2005-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A full-sized chevron was discovered on the first tail vertebra of "Sue", an extremely robust individual, indicating that this feature could not be used to differentiate the two morphs anyway. As
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      specimens have been found from
      <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">
       Saskatchewan
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">
       New Mexico
      </a>
      , differences between individuals may be indicative of geographic variation rather than sexual dimorphism. The differences could also be age-related, with 'robust' individuals being older animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brochu2003_21-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-brochu2003-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Only a single
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      specimen has been conclusively shown to belong to a specific sex. Examination of "B-rex" demonstrated the preservation of
      <a href="/wiki/Soft_tissue" title="Soft tissue">
       soft tissue
      </a>
      within several bones. Some of this tissue has been identified as a medullary tissue, a specialized tissue grown only in modern birds as a source of
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">
       calcium
      </a>
      for the production of
      <a href="/wiki/Eggshell" title="Eggshell">
       eggshell
      </a>
      during
      <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">
       ovulation
      </a>
      . As only female birds lay eggs, medullary tissue is only found naturally in females, although males are capable of producing it when injected with female reproductive
      <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">
       hormones
      </a>
      like
      <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">
       estrogen
      </a>
      . This strongly suggests that "B-rex" was female, and that she died during ovulation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schweitzeretal2005_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-schweitzeretal2005-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recent research has shown that medullary tissue is never found in crocodiles, which are thought to be the closest living relatives of dinosaurs, aside from birds. The shared presence of medullary tissue in birds and theropod dinosaurs is further evidence of the close
      <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">
       evolutionary
      </a>
      relationship between the two.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schweitzeretal2007_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-schweitzeretal2007-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Posture">
       Posture
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T._rex_old_posture.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="700" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/T._rex_old_posture.jpg/220px-T._rex_old_posture.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/T._rex_old_posture.jpg/330px-T._rex_old_posture.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/T._rex_old_posture.jpg/440px-T._rex_old_posture.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T._rex_old_posture.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Outdated reconstruction (by
        <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Knight" title="Charles R. Knight">
         Charles R. Knight
        </a>
        ), showing upright pose
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Modern representations in museums, art, and film show
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      with its body approximately parallel to the ground and tail extended behind the body to balance the head.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paul1988_29-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-paul1988-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Like many
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipedal" title="Bipedal">
       bipedal
      </a>
      dinosaurs,
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      was historically depicted as a 'living tripod', with the body at 45 degrees or less from the vertical and the tail dragging along the ground, similar to a
      <a href="/wiki/Kangaroo" title="Kangaroo">
       kangaroo
      </a>
      . This concept dates from
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Leidy" title="Joseph Leidy">
       Joseph Leidy
      </a>
      's 1865 reconstruction of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurus" title="Hadrosaurus">
        Hadrosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      , the first to depict a dinosaur in a bipedal posture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leidy1865_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-leidy1865-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1915, convinced that the creature stood upright,
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fairfield_Osborn" title="Henry Fairfield Osborn">
       Henry Fairfield Osborn
      </a>
      , former president of the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">
       American Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      , further reinforced the notion in unveiling the first complete
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      skeleton arranged this way. It stood in an upright pose for 77 years, until it was dismantled in 1992.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnhsite_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-amnhsite-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By 1970, scientists realized this pose was incorrect and could not have been maintained by a living animal, as it would have resulted in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dislocation_(medicine)" title="Dislocation (medicine)">
       dislocation
      </a>
      or weakening of several
      <a href="/wiki/Joint" title="Joint">
       joints
      </a>
      , including the hips and the articulation between the head and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_column" title="Spinal column">
       spinal column
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newman1970_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-newman1970-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The inaccurate AMNH mount inspired similar depictions in many films and paintings (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_F._Zallinger" title="Rudolph F. Zallinger">
       Rudolph Zallinger
      </a>
      's famous mural
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Reptiles" title="The Age of Reptiles">
        The Age of Reptiles
       </a>
      </i>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">
       Yale University
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Peabody Museum of Natural History">
       Peabody Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      )
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      until the 1990s, when films such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(film)" title="Jurassic Park (film)">
        Jurassic Park
       </a>
      </i>
      introduced a more accurate posture to the general public.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Arms">
       Arms
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_resting_pose.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="513" data-file-width="741" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Tyrannosaurus_resting_pose.jpg/220px-Tyrannosaurus_resting_pose.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Tyrannosaurus_resting_pose.jpg/330px-Tyrannosaurus_resting_pose.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Tyrannosaurus_resting_pose.jpg/440px-Tyrannosaurus_resting_pose.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_resting_pose.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The forelimbs might have been used to help
        <i>
         T.rex
        </i>
        rise from a resting pose, as seen in this cast ("Bucky" specimen)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      When
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      was first discovered, the
      <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">
       humerus
      </a>
      was the only element of the forelimb known.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborn1905_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-osborn1905-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For the initial mounted skeleton as seen by the public in 1915, Osborn substituted longer, three-fingered forelimbs like those of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">
        Allosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborn1917_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-osborn1917-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, a year earlier,
      <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Lambe" title="Lawrence Lambe">
       Lawrence Lambe
      </a>
      described the short, two-fingered forelimbs of the closely related
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Gorgosaurus" title="Gorgosaurus">
        Gorgosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lambe1914_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lambe1914-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This strongly suggested that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      had similar forelimbs, but this
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">
       hypothesis
      </a>
      was not confirmed until the first complete
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      forelimbs were identified in 1989, belonging to MOR 555 (the "Wankel rex").
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hornerlessem1993_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hornerlessem1993-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The remains of "Sue" also include complete forelimbs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brochu2003_21-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-brochu2003-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      arms are very small relative to overall body size, measuring only 1 metre (3.3ft) long, and some scholars have labelled them as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestigial_organs" title="Vestigial organs">
       vestigial
      </a>
      . However, the bones show large areas for
      <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">
       muscle
      </a>
      attachment, indicating considerable strength. This was recognized as early as 1906 by Osborn, who speculated that the forelimbs may have been used to grasp a mate during
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copulation" title="Copulation">
       copulation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborn1906_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-osborn1906-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has also been suggested that the forelimbs were used to assist the animal in rising from a prone position.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newman1970_64-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-newman1970-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaur_arm_104.JPG">
