        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1221" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Tyrannosaur_arm_104.JPG/220px-Tyrannosaur_arm_104.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Tyrannosaur_arm_104.JPG/330px-Tyrannosaur_arm_104.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Tyrannosaur_arm_104.JPG/440px-Tyrannosaur_arm_104.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaur_arm_104.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Diagram illustrating arm anatomy
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Another possibility is that the forelimbs held struggling prey while it was killed by the tyrannosaur's enormous jaws. This hypothesis may be supported by
      <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">
       biomechanical
      </a>
      analysis.
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      forelimb bones exhibit extremely thick
      <a href="/wiki/Bone#Structure" title="Bone">
       cortical bone
      </a>
      , which have been interpreted as evidence that they were developed to withstand heavy loads. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biceps_brachii" title="Biceps brachii">
       biceps brachii
      </a>
      muscle of a full-grown
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      was capable of lifting 199 kilograms (439lb) by itself; other muscles such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brachialis" title="Brachialis">
       brachialis
      </a>
      would work along with the biceps to make elbow flexion even more powerful. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M._biceps" title="M. biceps">
       M. biceps
      </a>
      muscle of
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      was 3.5 times as powerful as the
      <a href="/wiki/Human_equivalent" title="Human equivalent">
       human equivalent
      </a>
      . A
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      forearm had a limited range of motion, with the shoulder and elbow joints allowing only 40 and 45 degrees of motion, respectively. In contrast, the same two joints in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Deinonychus" title="Deinonychus">
        Deinonychus
       </a>
      </i>
      allow up to 88 and 130 degrees of motion, respectively, while a human arm can rotate 360 degrees at the shoulder and move through 165 degrees at the elbow. The heavy build of the arm bones, strength of the muscles, and limited range of motion may indicate a system evolved to hold fast despite the stresses of a struggling prey animal. In the first detailed scientific description of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      forelimbs, paleontologists Kenneth Carpenter and Matt Smith dismissed notions that the forelimbs were useless or that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      was an obligate scavenger.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpentersmith2001_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carpentersmith2001-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Soft_tissue">
       Soft tissue
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In the March 2005 issue of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">
        Science
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mary_Higby_Schweitzer" title="Mary Higby Schweitzer">
       Mary Higby Schweitzer
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_State_University" title="North Carolina State University">
       North Carolina State University
      </a>
      and colleagues announced the recovery of soft tissue from the marrow cavity of a fossilized leg bone, from a
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      . The bone had been intentionally, though reluctantly, broken for shipping and then not preserved in the normal manner, specifically because Schweitzer was hoping to test it for soft tissue.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithsonian-fields_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-smithsonian-fields-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Designated as the Museum of the Rockies specimen 1125, or MOR 1125, the dinosaur was previously excavated from the
      <a href="/wiki/Hell_Creek_Formation" title="Hell Creek Formation">
       Hell Creek Formation
      </a>
      . Flexible, bifurcating
      <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">
       blood vessels
      </a>
      and fibrous but elastic
      <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">
       bone
      </a>
      matrix tissue were recognized. In addition, microstructures resembling
      <a href="/wiki/Blood_cell" title="Blood cell">
       blood cells
      </a>
      were found inside the matrix and vessels. The structures bear resemblance to
      <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">
       ostrich
      </a>
      blood cells and vessels. Whether an unknown process, distinct from normal fossilization, preserved the material, or the material is original, the researchers do not know, and they are careful not to make any claims about preservation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MHSetalb_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MHSetalb-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      If it is found to be original material, any surviving proteins may be used as a means of indirectly guessing some of the DNA content of the dinosaurs involved, because each protein is typically created by a specific gene. The absence of previous finds may be the result of people assuming preserved tissue was impossible, therefore not looking. Since the first, two more tyrannosaurs and a hadrosaur have also been found to have such tissue-like structures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithsonian-fields_73-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-smithsonian-fields-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Research on some of the tissues involved has suggested that birds are closer relatives to tyrannosaurs than other modern animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_peptides.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1739" data-file-width="1603" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tyrannosaurus_peptides.jpg/170px-Tyrannosaurus_peptides.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tyrannosaurus_peptides.jpg/255px-Tyrannosaurus_peptides.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tyrannosaurus_peptides.jpg/340px-Tyrannosaurus_peptides.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_peptides.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         T.rex
        </i>
        femur (MOR 1125) from which demineralized matrix and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peptides" title="Peptides">
         peptides
        </a>
        (insets) were obtained
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In studies reported in the journal
      <i>
       Science
      </i>
      in April 2007, Asara and colleagues concluded that seven traces of
      <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">
       collagen
      </a>
      proteins detected in purified
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      bone most closely match those reported in
      <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">
       chickens
      </a>
      , followed by frogs and newts. The discovery of proteins from a creature tens of millions of years old, along with similar traces the team found in a mastodon bone at least 160,000 years old, upends the conventional view of fossils and may shift paleontologists' focus from bone hunting to biochemistry. Until these finds, most scientists presumed that fossilization replaced all living tissue with inert minerals. Paleontologist Hans Larsson of McGill University in Montreal, who was not part of the studies, called the finds "a milestone", and suggested that dinosaurs could "enter the field of molecular biology and really slingshot paleontology into the modern world".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Subsequent studies in April 2008 confirmed the close connection of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      to modern birds. Postdoctoral biology researcher Chris Organ at
      <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">
       Harvard University
      </a>
      announced, "With more data, they would probably be able to place
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      on the evolutionary tree between
      <a href="/wiki/Alligator" title="Alligator">
       alligators
      </a>
      and chickens and
      <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">
       ostriches
      </a>
      ." Co-author John M. Asara added, "We also show that it groups better with birds than modern reptiles, such as alligators and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anole_lizard" title="Anole lizard">
       green anole lizards
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The presumed soft tissue was called into question by Thomas Kaye of the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">
       University of Washington
      </a>
      and his co-authors in 2008. They contend that what was really inside the tyrannosaur bone was slimy
      <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">
       biofilm
      </a>
      created by bacteria that coated the voids once occupied by blood vessels and cells.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The researchers found that what previously had been identified as remnants of blood cells, because of the presence of iron, were actually
      <a href="/wiki/Framboid" title="Framboid">
       framboids
      </a>
      , microscopic mineral spheres bearing iron. They found similar spheres in a variety of other fossils from various periods, including an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonite" title="Ammonite">
       ammonite
      </a>
      . In the ammonite they found the spheres in a place where the iron they contain could not have had any relationship to the presence of blood.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, Schweitzer has strongly criticized Kaye's claims and argues that there's no reported evidence that biofilms can produce branching, hollow tubes like those noted in her study.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      San Antonio, Schweitzer and colleagues published an analysis in 2011 of what parts of the collagen had been recovered, finding that it was the inner parts of the collagen coil that had been preserved, as would have been expected from a long period of protein degradation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other research challenges the identification of soft tissue as biofilm and confirms finding "branching, vessel-like structures" from within fossilized bone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thermoregulation">
       Thermoregulation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Physiology_of_dinosaurs" title="Physiology of dinosaurs">
       Physiology of dinosaurs
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      As of 2014, it is not clear if
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">
       endothermic
      </a>
      (warm-blooded).
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , like most dinosaurs, was long thought to have an
      <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">
       ectothermic
      </a>
      ("cold-blooded") reptilian
      <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">
       metabolism
      </a>
      . The idea of dinosaur ectothermy was challenged by scientists like
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._Bakker" title="Robert T. Bakker">
       Robert T. Bakker
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Ostrom" title="John Ostrom">
       John Ostrom
      </a>
      in the early years of the "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinosaur_Renaissance" title="Dinosaur Renaissance">
       Dinosaur Renaissance
      </a>
      ", beginning in the late 1960s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1968_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bakker1968-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1972_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bakker1972-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      itself was claimed to have been
      <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">
       endothermic
      </a>
      ("warm-blooded"), implying a very active lifestyle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1986_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-bakker1986-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since then, several paleontologists have sought to determine the ability of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">
       regulate
      </a>
      its body temperature. Histological evidence of high growth rates in young
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      , comparable to those of mammals and birds, may support the hypothesis of a high metabolism. Growth curves indicate that, as in mammals and birds,
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      growth was limited mostly to immature animals, rather than the
      <a href="/wiki/Indeterminate_growth" title="Indeterminate growth">
       indeterminate growth
      </a>
      seen in most other
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">
       vertebrates
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hornerpadian2004_52-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hornerpadian2004-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_oxygen" title="Isotopes of oxygen">
       Oxygen isotope
      </a>
      ratios in fossilized bone are sometimes used to determine the temperature at which the bone was deposited, as the ratio between certain isotopes correlates with temperature. In one specimen, the isotope ratios in bones from different parts of the body indicated a temperature difference of no more than 4 to 5C (7 to 9F) between the vertebrae of the torso and the
      <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">
       tibia
      </a>
      of the lower leg. This small temperature range between the body core and the extremities was claimed by paleontologist Reese Barrick and
      <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">
       geochemist
      </a>
      William Showers to indicate that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      maintained a constant internal body temperature (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeotherm" title="Homeotherm">
       homeothermy
      </a>
      ) and that it enjoyed a metabolism somewhere between ectothermic reptiles and endothermic mammals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrettshowers1994_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-barrettshowers1994-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other scientists have pointed out that the ratio of oxygen isotopes in the fossils today does not necessarily represent the same ratio in the distant past, and may have been altered during or after fossilization (
      <a href="/wiki/Diagenesis" title="Diagenesis">
       diagenesis
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-truemanetal2003_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-truemanetal2003-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Barrick and Showers have defended their conclusions in subsequent papers, finding similar results in another theropod dinosaur from a different continent and tens of millions of years earlier in time (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Giganotosaurus" title="Giganotosaurus">
        Giganotosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrickshowers1999_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-barrickshowers1999-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">
       Ornithischian
      </a>
      dinosaurs also showed evidence of homeothermy, while
      <a href="/wiki/Varanidae" title="Varanidae">
       varanid
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">
       lizards
      </a>
      from the same formation did not.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrickstevens1997_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-barrickstevens1997-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even if
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      does exhibit evidence of homeothermy, it does not necessarily mean that it was endothermic. Such thermoregulation may also be explained by
      <a href="/wiki/Gigantothermy" title="Gigantothermy">
       gigantothermy
      </a>
      , as in some living
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">
       sea turtles
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paladinoetal1997_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-paladinoetal1997-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chinsamyhillenius2004_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-chinsamyhillenius2004-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Footprints">
       Footprints
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philmont_Scout_Ranch_Tyrannosaurus_footprint.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1482" data-file-width="1797" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Philmont_Scout_Ranch_Tyrannosaurus_footprint.jpg/220px-Philmont_Scout_Ranch_Tyrannosaurus_footprint.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Philmont_Scout_Ranch_Tyrannosaurus_footprint.jpg/330px-Philmont_Scout_Ranch_Tyrannosaurus_footprint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Philmont_Scout_Ranch_Tyrannosaurus_footprint.jpg/440px-Philmont_Scout_Ranch_Tyrannosaurus_footprint.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philmont_Scout_Ranch_Tyrannosaurus_footprint.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Probable footprint from
        <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">
         New Mexico
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Two isolated fossilized
      <a href="/wiki/Footprint" title="Footprint">
       footprints
      </a>
      have been tentatively assigned to
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      . The first was discovered at
      <a href="/wiki/Philmont_Scout_Ranch" title="Philmont Scout Ranch">
       Philmont Scout Ranch
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">
       New Mexico
      </a>
      , in 1983 by American geologist Charles Pillmore. Originally thought to belong to a
      <a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurid" title="Hadrosaurid">
       hadrosaurid
      </a>
      , examination of the footprint revealed a large 'heel' unknown in
      <a href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">
       ornithopod
      </a>
      dinosaur tracks, and traces of what may have been a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hallux" title="Hallux">
       hallux
      </a>
      , the dewclaw-like fourth digit of the tyrannosaur foot. The footprint was published as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichnogenus" title="Ichnogenus">
       ichnogenus
      </a>
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrannosauripus_pillmorei" title="Tyrannosauripus pillmorei">
        Tyrannosauripus pillmorei
       </a>
      </i>
      in 1994, by
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lockley" title="Martin Lockley">
       Martin Lockley
      </a>
      and Adrian Hunt. Lockley and Hunt suggested that it was very likely the track was made by a
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      , which would make it the first known footprint from this species. The track was made in what was once a vegetated wetland mud flat. It measures 83 centimetres (33in) long by 71 centimetres (28in) wide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lockley.26hunt1994_91-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lockley.26hunt1994-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A second footprint that may have been made by a
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was first reported in 2007 by British paleontologist Phil Manning, from the
      <a href="/wiki/Hell_Creek_Formation" title="Hell Creek Formation">
       Hell Creek Formation
      </a>
      of Montana. This second track measures 72 centimetres (28in) long, shorter than the track described by Lockley and Hunt. Whether or not the track was made by
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      is unclear, though
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Nanotyrannus
      </i>
      are the only large theropods known to have existed in the Hell Creek Formation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rextrack2007_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rextrack2007-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manningetal2008_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-manningetal2008-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion">
       Locomotion
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      There are two main issues concerning the locomotory abilities of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      : how well it could turn; and what its maximum straight-line speed was likely to have been. Both are relevant to the debate about whether it was a hunter or a scavenger.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      may have been slow to turn, possibly taking one to two seconds to turn only 45an amount that humans, being vertically oriented and tailless, can spin in a fraction of a second.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The cause of the difficulty is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_inertia" title="Rotational inertia">
       rotational inertia
      </a>
      , since much of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus'
      </i>
      mass was some distance from its center of gravity, like a human carrying a heavy timberalthough it might have reduced the average distance by arching its back and tail and pulling its head and forelimbs close to its body, rather like the way ice skaters pull their arms closer in order to spin faster.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarrierWalterLee2000TurningPerformance_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CarrierWalterLee2000TurningPerformance-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurid_trackway.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1442" data-file-width="1966" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Tyrannosaurid_trackway.png/220px-Tyrannosaurid_trackway.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Tyrannosaurid_trackway.png/330px-Tyrannosaurid_trackway.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Tyrannosaurid_trackway.png/440px-Tyrannosaurid_trackway.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurid_trackway.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Only known tyrannosaurid trackway (
        <i>
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bellatoripes_fredlundi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bellatoripes fredlundi (page does not exist)">
          Bellatoripes fredlundi
         </a>
        </i>
        ), from the
        <a href="/wiki/Wapiti_Formation" title="Wapiti Formation">
         Wapiti Formation
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">
         British Columbia
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Scientists have produced a wide range of maximum speed estimates, mostly around 11 metres per second (40km/h; 25mph), but a few as low as 511 metres per second (1840km/h; 1125mph), and a few as high as 20 metres per second (72km/h; 45mph). Researchers have to rely on various estimating techniques because, while there are many
      <a href="/wiki/Trackway" title="Trackway">
       tracks
      </a>
      of very large theropods walking, so far none have been found of very large theropods runningand this absence
      <i>
       may
      </i>
      indicate that they did not run.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson2004_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson2004-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Scientists who think that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was able to run point out that hollow bones and other features that would have lightened its body may have kept adult weight to a mere 4.5 metric tons (5.0 short tons) or so, or that other animals like
      <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">
       ostriches
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">
       horses
      </a>
      with long, flexible legs are able to achieve high speeds through slower but longer strides. Additionally, some have argued that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      had relatively larger leg muscles than any animal alive today, which could have enabled fast running 4070 kilometres per hour (2543mph).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb" style="float:left; clear:left; margin-right:5px; border:solid #ccc 1px; padding:2px; width:208px;;">
      <div class="thumbinner image" style="position:relative; width:200px; height:180px; border:solid #ccc 1px; background-color:white;">
       <div style=";left:5px; top:0px; width:103px; position:absolute;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png" title="Skeletal anatomy of a T. rex right leg">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="590" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png/103px-T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png/155px-T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png/206px-T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png 2x" width="103"/>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div style="text-align:left; background-color:transparent; font-size:12px; line-height:14px;">
        <div id="annotation_53x25" style="position:absolute; left:53px; top:25px; line-height:110%">
         <span style="background-color:transparent;">
          <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">
           Femur
          </a>
          (thigh bone)
         </span>
        </div>
        <div id="annotation_63x90" style="position:absolute; left:63px; top:90px; line-height:110%">
         <span style="background-color:transparent;">
          <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">
           Tibia
          </a>
          (shin bone)
         </span>
        </div>
        <div id="annotation_70x130" style="position:absolute; left:70px; top:130px; line-height:110%">
         <span style="background-color:transparent;">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metatarsal" title="Metatarsal">
           Metatarsals
          </a>
          (foot bones)
         </span>
        </div>
        <div id="annotation_2x148" style="position:absolute; left:2px; top:148px; line-height:110%">
         <span style="background-color:transparent;">
          <a href="/wiki/Dewclaw" title="Dewclaw">
           Dewclaw
          </a>
         </span>
        </div>
        <div id="annotation_65x165" style="position:absolute; left:65px; top:165px; line-height:110%">
         <span style="background-color:transparent;">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanges" title="Phalanges">
           Phalanges
          </a>
          (toe bones)
         </span>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="visibility:hidden">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="590" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png/103px-T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png/155px-T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png/206px-T_Rex_Leg_Bones.png 2x" width="103"/>
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumbcaption" style="width:200px;">
       Skeletal anatomy of a
       <i>
        T. rex
       </i>
       right leg
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Jack Horner and Don Lessem argued in 1993 that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was slow and probably could not run (no airborne phase in mid-stride), because its ratio of femur (thigh bone) to tibia (shin bone) length was greater than 1, as in most large theropods and like a modern
      <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">
       elephant
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hornerlessem1993_70-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hornerlessem1993-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, Holtz (1998) noted that tyrannosaurids and some closely related groups had significantly longer
      <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Proximal_and_distal" title="Anatomical terms of location">
       distal
      </a>
      hindlimb components (shin plus foot plus toes) relative to the femur length than most other theropods, and that tyrannosaurids and their close relatives had a tightly interlocked
      <a href="/wiki/Metatarsus" title="Metatarsus">
       metatarsus
      </a>
      that more effectively transmitted locomotory forces from the foot to the lower leg than in earlier theropods ("metatarsus" means the foot bones, which function as part of the leg in
      <a href="/wiki/Digitigrade" title="Digitigrade">
       digitigrade
      </a>
      animals). He therefore concluded that tyrannosaurids and their close relatives were the fastest large theropods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtz1998TaxonomyCoelurosauria_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Holtz1998TaxonomyCoelurosauria-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thomas Holtz Jr. would echo these sentiments in his 2013 lecture, stating that the giant allosaurs had shorter feet for the same body size than
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , whereas
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      had longer, skinnier and more interlocked feet for the same body size; attributes of faster moving animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HoltzLecture2013_56-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-HoltzLecture2013-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T._rex_versus_A._atrox_feet.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="347" data-file-width="502" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/T._rex_versus_A._atrox_feet.JPG/220px-T._rex_versus_A._atrox_feet.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/T._rex_versus_A._atrox_feet.JPG/330px-T._rex_versus_A._atrox_feet.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/T._rex_versus_A._atrox_feet.JPG/440px-T._rex_versus_A._atrox_feet.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T._rex_versus_A._atrox_feet.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         T. rex
        </i>
        foot showing the compressed
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctometatarsalian" title="Arctometatarsalian">
         arctometatarsalian
        </a>
        condition of the middle
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metatarsal" title="Metatarsal">
         metatarsal
        </a>
        , compared to that of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">
          Allosaurus
         </a>
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A study by Eric Snively and Anthony P Russel published on 2003 would also find that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurid" title="Tyrannosaurid">
       tyrannosaurid
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Arctometatarsal" title="Arctometatarsal">
       arctometatarsals
      </a>
      , and the and its elastic ligaments worked together in what he called a 'tensile keystone model' to strengthen the feet of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , increase the animals stability and add greater resistance to dissociation over that of other theropod families; while still allowing resiliency that is otherwise reduced in
      <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">
       ratites
      </a>
      , horses, giraffids and other animals with metapodia to a single element. The study would also point out that elastic ligaments in larger vertebrates could store and return relatively more elastic strain energy, which could have improved locomotor efficiency and decrease the strain energy transferred to the bones. The study would suggest that this mechanism could have worked efficiently in tyrannosaurids as well. Hence, the study involved identifying the type of ligaments attached to the metatarsals, then how they functioned together and comparing it to those of other theropods and modern day analogs. The scientists would find that arctometatarsals may have enabled tyrannosaurid feet to absorb forces such as linear deceleration, lateral acceleration and torsion more effectively than those of other theropods. It is also stated in their study that this may imply, though not demonstrate, that tyrannosaurids such as
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      had greater agility than other large theropods without an arctometatarsus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Christiansen (1998) estimated that the leg bones of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      were not significantly stronger than those of elephants, which are relatively limited in their top speed and never actually run (there is no airborne phase), and hence proposed that the dinosaur's maximum speed would have been about 11 metres per second (40km/h; 25mph), which is about the speed of a human sprinter. But he also noted that such estimates depend on many dubious assumptions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christiansen1998Strength_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christiansen1998Strength-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Farlow and colleagues (1995) have argued that a
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      weighing 5.4 metric tons (6.0 short tons) to 7.3 metric tons (8.0 short tons) would have been critically or even fatally injured if it had fallen while moving quickly, since its torso would have slammed into the ground at a deceleration of 6
      <i>
       g
      </i>
      (six times the acceleration due to gravity, or about 60meters/s) and its tiny arms could not have reduced the impact.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farlowetal1995_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-farlowetal1995-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However,
      <a href="/wiki/Giraffe" title="Giraffe">
       giraffes
      </a>
      have been known to gallop at 50 kilometres per hour (31mph), despite the risk that they might break a leg or worse, which can be fatal even in a "safe" environment such as a zoo.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus it is possible that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      also moved fast when necessary and had to accept such risks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander2006DinoBioMechanics_103-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Alexander2006DinoBioMechanics-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanna2002MultipleInjuriesBigAl_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hanna2002MultipleInjuriesBigAl-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_Rey.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2330" data-file-width="2055" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Tyrannosaurus_Rey.jpg/170px-Tyrannosaurus_Rey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Tyrannosaurus_Rey.jpg/255px-Tyrannosaurus_Rey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Tyrannosaurus_Rey.jpg/340px-Tyrannosaurus_Rey.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_Rey.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Leg mass of
        <i>
         T.rex
        </i>
        and a chicken compared, by
        <a href="/wiki/Luis_Rey" title="Luis Rey">
         Luis Rey
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In a study published by Gregory S. Paul in the journal
      <i>
       Gaia
      </i>
      , he would point out that the flexed kneed and
      <a href="/wiki/Digitigrade" title="Digitigrade">
       digitigrade
      </a>
      adult
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      were much better designed for running than elephants or humans, pointing out that Tyrannosaurus had a large
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilium_bone" title="Ilium bone">
       ilium bone
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cnemial_crest" title="Cnemial crest">
       cnemial crest
      </a>
      that would have supported large muscles needed for running. He would also mention that Alexander's(1989) formula to calculate speed by bone strength was only partly reliable. He suggests that the formula is overly sensitive to bone length; making long bones artificially weak. He would also point out that the reduction of the risk of being wounded in combat may have been worth the risk of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      falling while running.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSPaul2000_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GSPaul2000-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most recent research on
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      locomotion does not support speeds faster than 40 kilometres per hour (25mph), i.e. moderate-speed running. For example, a 2002 paper in the journal
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">
        Nature
       </a>
      </i>
      used a mathematical model (validated by applying it to three living animals,
      <a href="/wiki/Alligator" title="Alligator">
       alligators
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">
       chickens
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">
       humans
      </a>
      ; additionally later eight more species including emus and ostriches
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson2004_96-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson2004-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ) to gauge the leg muscle mass needed for fast running (over 40km/h or 25mph).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow_97-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They found that proposed top speeds in excess of 40 kilometres per hour (25mph) were unfeasible, because they would require very large leg muscles (more than approximately 4086%of total body mass). Even moderately fast speeds would have required large leg muscles. This discussion is difficult to resolve, as it is unknown how large the leg muscles actually were in
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      . If they were smaller, only 18 kilometres per hour (11mph) walking/jogging might have been possible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow_97-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A study in 2007 used computer models to estimate running speeds, based on data taken directly from fossils, and claimed that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      had a top running speed of 8 metres per second (29km/h; 18mph). An average professional
      <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">
       football
      </a>
      (soccer) player would be slightly slower, while a human sprinter can reach 12 metres per second (43km/h; 27mph). These computer models predict a top speed of 17.8 metres per second (64km/h; 40mph) for a 3-kilogram (6.6lb)
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Compsognathus" title="Compsognathus">
        Compsognathus
       </a>
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SellersManning2007ProcRSocB_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SellersManning2007ProcRSocB-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (probably a juvenile individual).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-compysize_108-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-compysize-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_muscle_mass.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2686" data-file-width="1835" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Tyrannosaurus_muscle_mass.png/170px-Tyrannosaurus_muscle_mass.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Tyrannosaurus_muscle_mass.png/255px-Tyrannosaurus_muscle_mass.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Tyrannosaurus_muscle_mass.png/340px-Tyrannosaurus_muscle_mass.png 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_muscle_mass.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Muscle mass reconstruction of M. caudofemoralis longus
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      However in 2010, Scott Persons, a graduate student from the University of Alberta proposed that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus's
      </i>
      speed may have been enhanced by strong tail muscles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anrefjournal_109-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-anrefjournal-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He found that theropods such as T rex had certain muscle arrangements that are different from modern day birds and mammals but with some similarities to modern reptiles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arcosaurref_110-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-arcosaurref-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He concluded that the caudofemoralis muscles which link the tail bones and the upper leg bones could have assisted
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      in leg retraction and enhanced its running ability, agility and balance. The caudofemoralis muscle would have been a key muscle in femoral retraction; pulling back the leg at the femur.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anrefjournal_109-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-anrefjournal-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The study also found that theropod skeletons such as those of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus'
      </i>
      had adaptations (such as elevated
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transverse_processes" title="Transverse processes">
       transverse processes
      </a>
      in the tail vertebrae) to enable the growth of larger tail muscles and that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus's
      </i>
      tail muscle mass may have been underestimated by over 25 percent and perhaps as much as 45 percent. The caudofemoralis muscle was found to comprise 58 percent of the muscle mass in the tail of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      also had the largest absolute and relative caudofemoralis muscle mass out of the three extinct organisms in the study. This is because
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      also had additional adaptations to enable large tail muscles; the elongation of its tail's
      <a href="/wiki/Haemal_arch" title="Haemal arch">
       hemal arches
      </a>
      . According to Persons, the increase in tail muscle mass would have moved the center of mass closer to the hind quarters and hips which would have lessened the strain on the leg muscles to support its weight; improving its overall balance and agility. This would also have made the animal less front heavy, thus reducing rotational inertia. Persons also notes that the tail is also rich in tendons and septa which could have been stores of elastic energy, and thereby improved locomotive efficiency. Persons adds that this means that non-avian theropods actually had broader tails than previously depicted, as broad or broader laterally than dorsoventrally near the base.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anrefjournal_109-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-anrefjournal-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arcosaurref_110-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-arcosaurref-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Heinrich Mallison from Berlin's Museum of Natural History would also present a theory on 2011, suggesting that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      and many other dinosaurs may have achieved relatively high speeds through short rapid strides instead of the long strides employed by modern birds and mammals when running, likening their movement to power-walking. This, according to Mallison, would have been achievable irrespective of joint strength and lessened the need for additional muscle mass in the legs, particularly at the ankles. To support his theory, Mallison assessed the limbs of various dinosaurs and found that they were different from that of modern mammals and birds; having their stride length greatly limited by their skeletons, but also having relatively large muscles at the hindquarters. He would however find a few similarities between the musculature of dinosaurs and race-walkers; having less muscle mass in the ankles but more at the hindquarters. Mallison suggests that the differences between dinosaurs the extant mammals and birds would also have made equations to calculate speed from stride length inapplicable to dinosaurs. John Hutchinson however advised caution regarding this theory, suggesting that they must first look into dinosaur muscles to see how frequently they could have contracted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeinrichPowerwalkingNature_111-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HeinrichPowerwalkingNature-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DailyMailPowerwalk_112-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DailyMailPowerwalk-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Those who argue that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was incapable of running estimate the top speed of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      at about 17 kilometres per hour (11mph). This is still faster than its most likely prey species,
      <a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurid" title="Hadrosaurid">
       hadrosaurids
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">
       ceratopsians
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow_97-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, some advocates of the idea that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was a predator claim that tyrannosaur running speed is not important, since it may have been slow but still faster than its probable prey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning2008_113-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-manning2008-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thomas Holtz would also note that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      foot was proportionately longer than the animals it hunted; duckbilled dinosarus and horned dinosaurs
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HoltzLecture2013_56-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-HoltzLecture2013-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, Paul and Christiansen (2000) argued that at least the later ceratopsians had upright forelimbs and the larger species may have been as fast as
      <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">
       rhinos
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PaulChristiansen2000NeoceratopsianForelimbPosture_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PaulChristiansen2000NeoceratopsianForelimbPosture-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Healed
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      bite wounds on ceratopsian fossils are interpreted as evidence of attacks on living ceratopsians (see below). If the ceratopsians that lived alongside
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      were fast, that casts doubt on the argument that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      did not have to be fast to catch its prey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanna2002MultipleInjuriesBigAl_104-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hanna2002MultipleInjuriesBigAl-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Brain_and_senses">
       Brain and senses
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sue_TRex_Skull_Full_Frontal.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3520" data-file-width="2544" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sue_TRex_Skull_Full_Frontal.JPG/170px-Sue_TRex_Skull_Full_Frontal.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sue_TRex_Skull_Full_Frontal.JPG/255px-Sue_TRex_Skull_Full_Frontal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sue_TRex_Skull_Full_Frontal.JPG/340px-Sue_TRex_Skull_Full_Frontal.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sue_TRex_Skull_Full_Frontal.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The eye-sockets faced mainly forwards, giving it good
        <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">
         binocular vision
        </a>
        ("Sue" specimen).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A study conducted by
      <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Witmer" title="Lawrence Witmer">
       Lawrence Witmer
      </a>
      and Ryan Ridgely of Ohio University added detail to the known sensory abilities of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , finding that they shared the heightened sensory abilities of other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coelurosaur" title="Coelurosaur">
       coelurosaurs
      </a>
      , highlighting relatively rapid and coordinated eye and head movements, as well as an enhanced ability to sense low frequency sounds that would allow tyrannosaurs to track prey movements from long distances and an enhanced sense of smell.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witmer2009_115-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-witmer2009-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A study published by Kent Stevens of the University of Oregon concluded that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      had keen vision. By applying modified
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perimetry" title="Perimetry">
       perimetry
      </a>
      to facial reconstructions of several dinosaurs including
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , the study found that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurs
      </i>
      had a binocular range of 55 degrees, surpassing that of modern hawks, and had 13 times the visual acuity of a human, thereby surpassing the visual acuity of an eagle which is only 3.6 times that of a person. This would have allowed
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurs
      </i>
      to discern objects as far as 6km away, which is greater than the 1.6km that a human can see.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevens2006Binocular_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stevens2006Binocular-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaffe_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-jaffe-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stevenswebpage_117-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-stevenswebpage-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thomas Holtz Jr. would note that high depth perception of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      may have been due to the prey it had to hunt; noting that it had to hunt horned dinosaurs such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">
        Triceratops
       </a>
      </i>
      , armored dinosaurs such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ankylosaurus" title="Ankylosaurus">
        Ankylosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      and the duck billed dinosaurs may have had complex social behaviors. He would suggest that this made precision more crucial for
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      enabling it to, "get in, get that blow in and take it down." In contrast,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Acrocanthosaurus" title="Acrocanthosaurus">
        Acrocanthosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      had limited depth perception because they hunted large sauropods, which were relatively rare during the time of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HoltzLecture2013_56-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-HoltzLecture2013-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_brain_aus.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="516" data-file-width="947" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Tyrannosaurus_brain_aus.jpg/220px-Tyrannosaurus_brain_aus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Tyrannosaurus_brain_aus.jpg/330px-Tyrannosaurus_brain_aus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Tyrannosaurus_brain_aus.jpg/440px-Tyrannosaurus_brain_aus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_brain_aus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Cast of the braincase at the
        <a href="/wiki/Australian_Museum" title="Australian Museum">
         Australian Museum
        </a>
        , Sydney.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      had very large
      <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">
       olfactory bulbs
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_nerve" title="Olfactory nerve">
       olfactory nerves
      </a>
      relative to their brain size, the organs responsible for a heightened sense of smell. This suggests that the sense of smell was highly developed, and implies that tyrannosaurs could detect carcasses by scent alone across great distances. The sense of smell in tyrannosaurs may have been comparable to modern
      <a href="/wiki/Vulture" title="Vulture">
       vultures
      </a>
      , which use scent to track carcasses for scavenging. Research on the olfactory bulbs has shown that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      had the most highly developed sense of smell of 21 sampled non-avian dinosaurs species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calgary_Herald_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Calgary_Herald-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Somewhat unusually among theropods,
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      had very long
      <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">
       cochlea
      </a>
      of the ear. The length of the cochlea is often related to hearing acuity, or at least the importance of hearing in behavior, implying that hearing was a particularly important sense to tyrannosaurs. Specifically, data suggests that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      heard best in the low-frequency range, and that low-frequency sounds were an important part of tyrannosaur behavior.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witmer2009_115-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-witmer2009-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A study by Grant R. Hurlburt, Ryan C. Ridgely and Lawrence Witmer would obtain estimates for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encephalization_Quotient" title="Encephalization Quotient">
       Encephalization Quotients
      </a>
      (EQs), based on reptiles (REQs) and based on birds (BEQs), as well estimates for the ratio of cerebrum to brain mass. The study would conclude that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      had the relatively largest brain of all adult non-avian dinosaurs with the exception of certain small maniraptoriforms (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bambiraptor" title="Bambiraptor">
        Bambiraptor
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Troodon" title="Troodon">
        Troodon
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ornithomimus" title="Ornithomimus">
        Ornithomimus
       </a>
      </i>
      ). The study would however find that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      relative brain size was still within the range of modern reptiles, being at most 2 standard deviations (SDs) above the mean of non-avian reptiles log REQs. The estimates for the ratio of cerebrum mass to brain mass would range from 47.5 to 49.53 percent. According to the study, this is more than the lowest estimates for extant birds (44.6 percent), but still close to the typical ratios of the smallest sexually mature alligators which range from 45.947.9 percent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trexpaleobiologychapter_6_119-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Trexpaleobiologychapter_6-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding_strategies">
       Feeding strategies
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Feeding_behaviour_of_Tyrannosaurus" title="Feeding behaviour of Tyrannosaurus">
       Feeding behaviour of Tyrannosaurus
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lebendrekonstruktion_T.rex_Sch%C3%A4del_im_NHM_Wien.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2264" data-file-width="3349" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Lebendrekonstruktion_T.rex_Sch%C3%A4del_im_NHM_Wien.JPG/220px-Lebendrekonstruktion_T.rex_Sch%C3%A4del_im_NHM_Wien.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Lebendrekonstruktion_T.rex_Sch%C3%A4del_im_NHM_Wien.JPG/330px-Lebendrekonstruktion_T.rex_Sch%C3%A4del_im_NHM_Wien.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Lebendrekonstruktion_T.rex_Sch%C3%A4del_im_NHM_Wien.JPG/440px-Lebendrekonstruktion_T.rex_Sch%C3%A4del_im_NHM_Wien.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lebendrekonstruktion_T.rex_Sch%C3%A4del_im_NHM_Wien.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Bust in Naturhistorischen Museum Wien
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A 2012 study by scientists Karl Bates and Peter Falkingham suggested that the bite force of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      could have been the strongest of any terrestrial animal that has ever lived. The calculations suggested that adult
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      could have generated from 35,000 to 57,000 newtons of force in the back teeth, or the equivalent of three times the force estimated for a great white shark, 15 times the force of an African lion, 3 1/2 times the force of an Australian saltwater crocodile, 77 times the force of an adult human and around 7 times the estimated force for
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">
        Allosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FalkinghamBatesBiteforceSwitek2012_120-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FalkinghamBatesBiteforceSwitek2012-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BatesFalkingham2012_121-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BatesFalkingham2012-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, even higher estimates were made by professor Mason B. Meers of the University of Tampa in 2003. In his study, Meers estimated a possible bite force of around 183,000 to 235,000 newtons or 18.3 to 23.5 metric tons; a bite force equivalent to that of the largest Megalodon shark specimens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MM03_9-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-MM03-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bs_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-bs-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The debate about whether
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was a
      <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">
       predator
      </a>
      or a pure
      <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">
       scavenger
      </a>
      is as old as the debate about its locomotion. Lambe (1917) described a good skeleton of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      close relative
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Gorgosaurus" title="Gorgosaurus">
        Gorgosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      and concluded that it and therefore also
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was a pure scavenger, because the
      <i>
       Gorgosaurus
      </i>
      teeth showed hardly any wear.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambe1917Gorgosaurus_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lambe1917Gorgosaurus-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This argument is no longer taken seriously, because theropods replaced their teeth quite rapidly. Ever since the first discovery of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      most scientists have speculated that it was a predator; like modern large predators it would readily scavenge or steal another predator's kill if it had the opportunity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FarlowHoltz2002FossilRecordPredation_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FarlowHoltz2002FossilRecordPredation-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_tooth_marks.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2716" data-file-width="1568" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tyrannosaurus_tooth_marks.png/170px-Tyrannosaurus_tooth_marks.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tyrannosaurus_tooth_marks.png/255px-Tyrannosaurus_tooth_marks.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tyrannosaurus_tooth_marks.png/340px-Tyrannosaurus_tooth_marks.png 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_tooth_marks.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Tyrannosaurus
        </i>
        tooth marks on bones of various herbivores
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Paleontologist
      <a href="/wiki/Jack_Horner_(paleontologist)" title="Jack Horner (paleontologist)">
       Jack Horner
      </a>
      has been a major advocate of the idea that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was exclusively a scavenger and did not engage in active hunting at all,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hornerlessem1993_70-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-hornerlessem1993-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horner1994SteakKnives_125-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Horner1994SteakKnives-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2003TrexOnTrial_126-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBC2003TrexOnTrial-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though Horner himself has claimed that he never published this idea in the peer reviewed scientific literature and used it mainly as a tool to teach a popular audience, particularly children, the dangers of making assumptions in science (such as assuming
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      was a hunter) without using evidence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skepticsguideinterview_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-skepticsguideinterview-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nevertheless, Horner presented several arguments in the popular literature to support the pure scavenger hypothesis:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Tyrannosaur arms are short when compared to other known predators. Horner argues that the arms were too short to make the necessary gripping force to hold on to prey.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
        <a href="#cite_note-128">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         128
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Tyrannosaurs had large
       <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">
        olfactory bulbs
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_nerve" title="Olfactory nerve">
        olfactory nerves
       </a>
       (relative to their brain size). These suggest a highly developed sense of smell which could sniff out carcasses over great distances, as modern
       <a href="/wiki/Vulture" title="Vulture">
        vultures
       </a>
       do. Research on the olfactory bulbs of dinosaurs has shown that
       <i>
        Tyrannosaurus
       </i>
       had the most highly developed sense of smell of 21 sampled dinosaurs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
        <a href="#cite_note-129">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         129
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Opponents of the pure scavenger hypothesis have used the example of vultures in the opposite way, arguing that the scavenger hypothesis is implausible because the only modern pure scavengers are large gliding birds, which use their keen senses and energy-efficient gliding to cover vast areas economically.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlyModernScavengersBirds_130-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OnlyModernScavengersBirds-130">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         130
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, researchers from Glasgow concluded that an ecosystem as productive as the current
       <a href="/wiki/Serengeti" title="Serengeti">
        Serengeti
       </a>
       would provide sufficient
       <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">
        carrion
       </a>
       for a large theropod scavenger, although the theropod might have had to be cold-blooded in order to get more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calories" title="Calories">
        calories
       </a>
       from carrion than it spent on foraging (see
       <a href="/wiki/Physiology_of_dinosaurs#Metabolism" title="Physiology of dinosaurs">
        Metabolism of dinosaurs
       </a>
       ). They also suggested that modern ecosystems like Serengeti have no large terrestrial scavengers because gliding birds now do the job much more efficiently, while large theropods did not face competition for the scavenger
       <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">
        ecological niche
       </a>
       from gliding birds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuxtonHouston2003TRexScavenger_131-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RuxtonHouston2003TRexScavenger-131">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         131
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Tyrannosaur teeth could crush bone, and therefore could extract as much food (
       <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">
        bone marrow
       </a>
       ) as possible from carcass remnants, usually the least nutritious parts. Karen Chin and colleagues have found bone fragments in
       <a href="/wiki/Coprolite" title="Coprolite">
        coprolites
       </a>
       (fossilized feces) that they attribute to tyrannosaurs, but point out that a tyrannosaur's teeth were not well adapted to systematically chewing bone like
       <a href="/wiki/Hyena" title="Hyena">
        hyenas
       </a>
       do to extract marrow.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChinEtal1998KingSizeCoprolite_132-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ChinEtal1998KingSizeCoprolite-132">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         132
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Since at least some of
       <i>
        Tyrannosaurus
       </i>
       's potential prey could move quickly, evidence that it walked instead of ran could indicate that it was a scavenger.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horner1994SteakKnives_125-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Horner1994SteakKnives-125">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         125
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinodictionary_133-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dinodictionary-133">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         133
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       On the other hand, recent analyses suggest that
       <i>
        Tyrannosaurus
       </i>
       , while slower than large modern terrestrial predators, may well have been fast enough to prey on large
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadrosaurs" title="Hadrosaurs">
        hadrosaurs
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceratopsians" title="Ceratopsians">
        ceratopsians
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow_97-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonGarcia2002TrexSlow-97">
