         <span>
          [
         </span>
         97
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning2008_113-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-manning2008-113">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         113
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Other evidence suggests hunting behavior in
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      . The eye-sockets of tyrannosaurs are positioned so that the eyes would point forward, giving them
      <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">
       binocular vision
      </a>
      slightly better than that of modern
      <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">
       hawks
      </a>
      . Horner also pointed out that the tyrannosaur lineage had a history of steadily improving binocular vision. It is not obvious why
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">
       natural selection
      </a>
      would have favored this long-term trend if tyrannosaurs had been pure scavengers, which would not have needed the advanced
      <a href="/wiki/Depth_perception" title="Depth perception">
       depth perception
      </a>
      that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stereoscopic_vision" title="Stereoscopic vision">
       stereoscopic vision
      </a>
      provides.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevens2006Binocular_24-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stevens2006Binocular-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaffe_25-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-jaffe-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In modern animals, binocular vision is found mainly in predators.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DMNS_Edmontosaurus.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1220" data-file-width="1800" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/DMNS_Edmontosaurus.png/220px-DMNS_Edmontosaurus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/DMNS_Edmontosaurus.png/330px-DMNS_Edmontosaurus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/DMNS_Edmontosaurus.png/440px-DMNS_Edmontosaurus.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DMNS_Edmontosaurus.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The damage to the tail vertebrae of this
        <i>
         Edmontosaurus annectens
        </i>
        skeleton (on display at the Denver Museum of Nature and Science) indicates that it may have been bitten by a
        <i>
         Tyrannosaurus
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A skeleton of the hadrosaurid
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Edmontosaurus" title="Edmontosaurus">
        Edmontosaurus
       </a>
       annectens
      </i>
      has been described from Montana with healed tyrannosaur-inflicted damage on its tail
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">
       vertebrae
      </a>
      . The fact that the damage seems to have healed suggests that the
      <i>
       Edmontosaurus
      </i>
      survived a tyrannosaur's attack on a living target, i.e. the tyrannosaur had attempted active predation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter1998_134-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carpenter1998-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is also evidence for an aggressive interaction between a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">
        Triceratops
       </a>
      </i>
      and a
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      in the form of partially healed tyrannosaur tooth marks on a
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      brow horn and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squamosal" title="Squamosal">
       squamosal
      </a>
      (a bone of the
      <a href="/wiki/Neck_frill" title="Neck frill">
       neck frill
      </a>
      ); the bitten horn is also broken, with new bone growth after the break. It is not known what the exact nature of the interaction was, though: either animal could have been the aggressor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JH08_135-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JH08-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since the
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      wounds healed, it is most likely that the
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      survived the encounter and managed to overcome the
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      . Paleontologist
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dodson" title="Peter Dodson">
       Peter Dodson
      </a>
      estimates that in a battle against a bull
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      , the
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      had the upper hand and would successfully defend itself by inflicting fatal wounds to the
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      using its sharp horns.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When examining
      <a href="/wiki/Sue_(dinosaur)" title="Sue (dinosaur)">
       Sue
      </a>
      , paleontologist
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pete_Larson" title="Pete Larson">
       Pete Larson
      </a>
      found a broken and healed
      <a href="/wiki/Fibula" title="Fibula">
       fibula
      </a>
      and tail vertebrae, scarred facial bones and a tooth from another
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      embedded in a neck vertebra. If correct, these might be strong evidence for aggressive behavior between tyrannosaurs but whether it would have been competition for food and mates or active
      <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">
       cannibalism
      </a>
      is unclear.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TC98_137-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TC98-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, further recent investigation of these purported wounds has shown that most are infections rather than injuries (or simply damage to the fossil after death) and the few injuries are too general to be indicative of intraspecific conflict.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horner1994SteakKnives_125-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Horner1994SteakKnives-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some researchers argue that if
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      were a scavenger, another dinosaur had to be the top predator in the Amerasian Upper Cretaceous. Top prey were the larger
      <a href="/wiki/Marginocephalia" title="Marginocephalia">
       marginocephalians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">
       ornithopods
      </a>
      . The other tyrannosaurids share so many characteristics that only small
      <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">
       dromaeosaurs
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Troodontidae" title="Troodontidae">
       troodontids
      </a>
      remain as feasible top predators. In this light, scavenger hypothesis adherents have suggested that the size and power of tyrannosaurs allowed them to
      <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">
       steal kills
      </a>
      from smaller predators,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinodictionary_133-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dinodictionary-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although they may have had a hard time finding enough meat to scavenge, being outnumbered by smaller theropods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most paleontologists accept that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was both an active predator and a scavenger like most large carnivores.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B-rex_teeth.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="920" data-file-width="2728" height="74" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/B-rex_teeth.jpg/220px-B-rex_teeth.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/B-rex_teeth.jpg/330px-B-rex_teeth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/B-rex_teeth.jpg/440px-B-rex_teeth.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B-rex_teeth.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Two teeth from the lower jaw of specimen MOR 1125, "B-rex", showing the variation in tooth size within an individual
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      may have had infectious saliva used to kill its prey. This theory was first proposed by
      <a href="/wiki/William_Abler" title="William Abler">
       William Abler
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Abler examined the teeth of tyrannosaurids between each tooth serration; the serrations may have held pieces of carcass with bacteria, giving
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      a deadly, infectious bite much like the
      <a href="/wiki/Komodo_dragon" title="Komodo dragon">
       Komodo dragon
      </a>
      was thought to have. However, Jack Horner regards
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      tooth serrations as more like cubes in shape than the serrations on a Komodo monitor's teeth, which are rounded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      All forms of saliva contain possibly hazardous bacteria, so the prospect of it being used as a method of predation is disputable.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , and most other theropods, probably primarily processed carcasses with lateral shakes of the head, like crocodilians. The head was not as maneuverable as the skulls of
      <a href="/wiki/Allosauroidea" title="Allosauroidea">
       allosauroids
      </a>
      , due to flat joints of the neck vertebrae.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESetal2013_141-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ESetal2013-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cannibalism">
       Cannibalism
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      A study from Currie, Horner, Erickson and Longrich in 2010 has been put forward as evidence of cannibalism in the genus
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HornerCurrie_et.al._2010_Trex_cannibalism_142-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HornerCurrie_et.al._2010_Trex_cannibalism-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They studied some
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      specimens with tooth marks in the bones, attributable to the same genus. The tooth marks were identified in the
      <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">
       humerus
      </a>
      , foot bones and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metatarsal" title="Metatarsal">
       metatarsals
      </a>
      , and this was seen as evidence for opportunistic scavenging, rather than wounds caused by intraspecific combat. In a fight, they proposed it would be difficult to reach down to bite in the feet of a rival, making it more likely that the bitemarks were made in a carcass. As the bitemarks were made in body parts with relatively scantly amounts of flesh, it is suggested that the
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was feeding on a cadaver in which the more fleshy parts already had been consumed. They were also open to the possibility that other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurids" title="Tyrannosaurids">
       tyrannosaurids
      </a>
      practiced cannibalism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HornerCurrie_et.al._2010_Trex_cannibalism_142-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-HornerCurrie_et.al._2010_Trex_cannibalism-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pack_behavior">
       Pack behavior
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_Rex_Growth_Series.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2266" data-file-width="3616" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Tyrannosaurus_Rex_Growth_Series.jpg/220px-Tyrannosaurus_Rex_Growth_Series.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Tyrannosaurus_Rex_Growth_Series.jpg/330px-Tyrannosaurus_Rex_Growth_Series.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Tyrannosaurus_Rex_Growth_Series.jpg/440px-Tyrannosaurus_Rex_Growth_Series.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_Rex_Growth_Series.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Mounted skeletons of different age groups, Los Angeles Natural History Museum
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_J._Currie" title="Philip J. Currie">
       Philip J. Currie
      </a>
      of the University of Alberta has suggested that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      may have been pack animals. Currie compared
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      favorably to related species
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarbosaurus_bataar" title="Tarbosaurus bataar">
        Tarbosaurus bataar
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albertosaurus_sarcophagus" title="Albertosaurus sarcophagus">
        Albertosaurus sarcophagus
       </a>
      </i>
      , fossil evidence from which Currie had previously used to suggest that they lived in packs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discoverdinogangs_143-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-discoverdinogangs-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Currie pointed out that a find in South Dakota preserved three
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      skeletons in close proximity to each other.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tgraphpack_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-tgraphpack-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After using CT scanning, Currie stated that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      would have been capable of such complex behavior, because its brain size is three times greater than what would be expected for an animal of its size. Currie elaborated that
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      had a larger brain to body size proportion than crocodiles and three times more than plant eating dinosaurs such as
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      of the same size. Currie believed
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      to be six times smarter than most dinosaurs and other reptiles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discoverdinogangs_143-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-discoverdinogangs-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-djournal_145-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-djournal-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Currie stated that because the available prey, such as
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Ankylosaurus
      </i>
      , were well armored, and that others were fast moving, it would have been necessary for
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      to hunt in groups. Currie speculated that juveniles and adults would have hunted together, with the faster juveniles chasing down the prey and the more powerful adults making the kill, by analogy to modern-day pack hunters where each member contributes a skill.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discoverdinogangs_143-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-discoverdinogangs-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However, Currie's pack-hunting hypothesis has been harshly criticized by other scientists. Brian Switek, writing for
      <i>
       The Guardian
      </i>
      in 2011,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switek2011dinogangs_146-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-switek2011dinogangs-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      noted that Currie's pack hypothesis has not been presented as research in a peer-reviewed scientific journal, but primarily in relation to a television special and tie-in book called "Dino Gangs." Switek also noted that Currie's argument for pack hunting in
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      is primarily based on analogy to a different species,
      <i>
       Tarbosaurus bataar
      </i>
      , and that the supposed evidence for pack hunting in
      <i>
       T. bataar
      </i>
      itself has not yet been published and subjected to scientific scrutiny. According to Switek and other scientists who have participated in panel discussions about the "Dino Gangs" television program, the evidence for pack hunting in
      <i>
       Tarbosaurus
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Albertosaurus
      </i>
      is weak, based primarily on the association of several skeletons, for which numerous alternative explanations have been proposed (e.g. drought or floods forcing numerous specimens together to die in one place). In fact, Switek notes that the
      <i>
       Albertosaurus
      </i>
      bonebed site, on which Currie has based most of the interpretations of supposed pack hunting in related species, preserves geological evidence of just such a flood. Switek said, "bones alone are not enough to reconstruct dinosaur behaviour. The geological context in which those bones are found  the intricate details of ancient environments and the pace of prehistoric time  are essential to investigating the lives and deaths of dinosaurs,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switek2011dinogangs_146-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-switek2011dinogangs-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and noted that Currie must first describe the geological evidence from other tyrannosaur bonebed sites before jumping to conclusions about social behavior. Switek described the sensational claims provided in press releases and news stories surrounding the "Dino Gangs" program as "nauseating hype" and noted that the production company responsible for the program, Atlantic Productions, has a poor record involving exaggerating claims about new fossil discoveries, most notably the controversial claim it published regarding the supposed early human ancestor
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Darwinius" title="Darwinius">
        Darwinius
       </a>
      </i>
      , which soon turned out to be a relative of
      <a href="/wiki/Lemur" title="Lemur">
       lemurs
      </a>
      instead.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switek2011dinogangs_146-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-switek2011dinogangs-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Witmer" title="Lawrence Witmer">
       Lawrence Witmer
      </a>
      would also write about his opinions on Philip J. Currie's theory. He would write that social behavior can't be determined by brain endocasts and that the brains of solitary leopards are identical to those of a cooperatively hunting lion. He would state that the best they could do was look at the overall cerebral size, compare it to modern animals and make estimations. In his opinion, the brain of tyrannosaurs were large enough for communal hunting. Lawrence Witmer would elaborate that communal hunting is when each animal attacks as an individual but their collective attack benefits each of the individuals, whereas in social pack hunting, the individual would forgo immediate benefit knowing that it would benefit later on when another member of the pack has made a kill. Witmer states that in the continuum between solitary and cooperative hunting, communal hunting is in between. Witmer would call communal hunting as the step towards cooperative hunting. He would also state that there is nothing in the brain endocasts that would prove or disprove cooperative hunting. But he would also write that as for communal hunting; he would have difficulty accepting that tyrannosaurs wouldn't have exploited the opportunity to join others in making a kill because that would have decreased risk and increased the chance for success.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LawrenceWitmerdinogangs2011_147-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LawrenceWitmerdinogangs2011-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On July 23, 2014, evidence, for the first time, in the form of fossilized
      <a href="/wiki/Trackway" title="Trackway">
       trackways
      </a>
      in Canada, shows that tyrannosaurs may have hunted in groups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TG-20140724_148-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TG-20140724-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pathology">
       Pathology
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T._rex_infection.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1344" data-file-width="1797" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/T._rex_infection.png/220px-T._rex_infection.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/T._rex_infection.png/330px-T._rex_infection.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/T._rex_infection.png/440px-T._rex_infection.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T._rex_infection.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Restoration of an individual (based on
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOR_980" title="MOR 980">
         MOR 980
        </a>
        ) with parasite infections
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 2001,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bruce_Rothschild&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bruce Rothschild (page does not exist)">
       Bruce Rothschild
      </a>
      and others published a study examining evidence for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_fractures" title="Stress fractures">
       stress fractures
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tendon_avulsions" title="Tendon avulsions">
       tendon avulsions
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theropod" title="Theropod">
       theropod
      </a>
      dinosaurs and the implications for their behavior. Since stress fractures are caused by repeated trauma rather than singular events they are more likely to be caused by regular behavior than other types of injuries. Of the 81
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      foot bones examined in the study one was found to have a stress fracture, while none of the 10 hand bones were found to have stress fractures. The researchers found tendon avulsions only among
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">
        Allosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      . An avulsion injury left a divot on the humerus of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sue_the_T._rex" title="Sue the T. rex">
       Sue the
       <i>
        T.rex
       </i>
      </a>
      , apparently located at the origin of the
      <a href="/wiki/Deltoid_muscle" title="Deltoid muscle">
       deltoid
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teres_major" title="Teres major">
       teres major
      </a>
      muscles. The presence of avulsion injuries being limited to the forelimb and shoulder in both
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Allosaurus
      </i>
      suggests that theropods may have had a musculature more complex and functionally different from those of birds. The researchers concluded that Sue's tendon avulsion was probably obtained from struggling prey. The presence of stress fractures and tendon avulsions in general provide evidence for a "very active" predation-based diet rather than obligate scavenging.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rothschild-dino_150-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rothschild-dino-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A 2009 study showed that holes in the skulls of several specimens that were previously explained by intraspecific attacks might have been caused by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Trichomonas" title="Trichomonas">
        Trichomonas
       </a>
      </i>
      -like parasites that commonly infect
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aves" title="Aves">
       avians
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff_151-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wolff-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Further evidence of intraspecific attack would however be found by Joseph Peterson and his colleagues in the juvenile
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      nicknamed "Jane". Peterson and his team found that Jane's skull showed healed puncture wounds on the upper jaw and snout which they believe came from another juvenile
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      . Subsequent CT scans of Jane's skull would further confirm the team's hypothesis, showing that the puncture wounds came from a traumatic injury and that there was subsequent healing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JaneFacebiting_152-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JaneFacebiting-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The team would also state that Jane's injuries were structurally different from the parasite induced lesions found in Sue and that Jane's injuries were on her face whereas the parasite that infected Sue caused lesions to the lower jaw.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JaneFaceBiteWeb_153-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JaneFaceBiteWeb-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Paleoecology">
       Paleoecology
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hell_Creek_dinosaurs_and_pterosaurs_by_durbed.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2007" data-file-width="3380" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hell_Creek_dinosaurs_and_pterosaurs_by_durbed.jpg/220px-Hell_Creek_dinosaurs_and_pterosaurs_by_durbed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hell_Creek_dinosaurs_and_pterosaurs_by_durbed.jpg/330px-Hell_Creek_dinosaurs_and_pterosaurs_by_durbed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hell_Creek_dinosaurs_and_pterosaurs_by_durbed.jpg/440px-Hell_Creek_dinosaurs_and_pterosaurs_by_durbed.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hell_Creek_dinosaurs_and_pterosaurs_by_durbed.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Tyrannosaurus
        </i>
        and other animals of the Hell Creek Formation
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      lived during what is referred to as the
      <a href="/wiki/Lancian" title="Lancian">
       Lancian
      </a>
      faunal stage (Maastrichtian age) at the end of the Late Cretaceous.
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      ranged from Canada in the north to at least Texas and New Mexico in the south of Western North America. During this time
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      was the major herbivore in the northern portion of its range, while the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanosaurian" title="Titanosaurian">
       titanosaurian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">
       sauropod
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Alamosaurus" title="Alamosaurus">
        Alamosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      "dominated" its southern range.
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      remains have been discovered in different ecosystems, including inland and coastal sub-tropical, and semi-arid plains.
     </p>
     <p>
      Several notable
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      remains have been found in the
      <a href="/wiki/Hell_Creek_Formation" title="Hell Creek Formation">
       Hell Creek Formation
      </a>
      . During the Maastrichtian this area was subtropical, with a warm and humid climate. The flora consisted of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">
       angiosperms
      </a>
      , but also included trees like dawn redwood (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Metasequoia" title="Metasequoia">
        Metasequoia
       </a>
      </i>
      ) and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Araucaria_araucana" title="Araucaria araucana">
        Araucaria
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      shared this ecosystem with
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      , related
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceratopsian" title="Ceratopsian">
       ceratopsian
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Torosaurus" title="Torosaurus">
        Torosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      , hadrosaurid
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Edmontosaurus_annectens" title="Edmontosaurus annectens">
        Edmontosaurus annectens
       </a>
      </i>
      , armored dinosaur
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ankylosaurus" title="Ankylosaurus">
        Ankylosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pachycephalosaurus" title="Pachycephalosaurus">
        Pachycephalosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hypsilophodont" title="Hypsilophodont">
       hypsilophodont
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Thescelosaurus" title="Thescelosaurus">
        Thescelosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      , and the theropods
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ornithomimus" title="Ornithomimus">
        Ornithomimus
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Troodon" title="Troodon">
        Troodon
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another formation with tyrannosaur remains is the
      <a href="/wiki/Lance_Formation" title="Lance Formation">
       Lance Formation
      </a>
      of Wyoming. This has been interpenetrated as a
      <a href="/wiki/Bayou" title="Bayou">
       bayou
      </a>
      environment similar to today's Gulf Coast. The fauna was very similar to Hell Creek, but with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Struthiomimus" title="Struthiomimus">
        Struthiomimus
       </a>
      </i>
      replacing its relative
      <i>
       Ornithomimus
      </i>
      . Additionally, the small ceratopsian
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Leptoceratops" title="Leptoceratops">
        Leptoceratops
       </a>
      </i>
      also lived in the area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In its southern range
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      lived alongside
      <i>
       Alamosaurus
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       Torosaurus
      </i>
      , a species of
      <i>
       Edmontosaurus
      </i>
      , possibly the ankylosaur
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Glyptodontopelta" title="Glyptodontopelta">
        Glyptodontopelta
       </a>
      </i>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">
       pterosaur
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Quetzalcoatlus" title="Quetzalcoatlus">
        Quetzalcoatlus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
       <a href="#cite_note-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The region is interpenetrated to be semi-arid inland plains, probably following the retreat of the
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Interior_Seaway" title="Western Interior Seaway">
       Western Interior Seaway
      </a>
      as global sea levels fell.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
       <a href="#cite_note-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_skeleton.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="996" data-file-width="1738" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Tyrannosaurus_skeleton.jpg/220px-Tyrannosaurus_skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Tyrannosaurus_skeleton.jpg/330px-Tyrannosaurus_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Tyrannosaurus_skeleton.jpg/440px-Tyrannosaurus_skeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus_skeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Skeletal restoration by William D. Matthew from 1905, the first reconstruction of this dinosaur ever published
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LindaHall_158-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-LindaHall-158">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          158
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fairfield_Osborn" title="Henry Fairfield Osborn">
       Henry Fairfield Osborn
      </a>
      , president of the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">
       American Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      , named
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      in 1905. The generic name is derived from the
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      words
      <i>
       
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       tyrannos
      </i>
      , meaning "tyrant") and
      <i>
       
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       sauros
      </i>
      , meaning "lizard"). Osborn used the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
       Latin
      </a>
      word
      <i>
       rex
      </i>
      , meaning "king", for the specific name. The full
      <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">
       binomial
      </a>
      therefore translates to "tyrant lizard the king" or "King Tyrant Lizard",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
       <a href="#cite_note-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      emphasizing the animal's size and perceived dominance over other species of the time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborn1905_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-osborn1905-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_finds">
       Earliest finds
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dynamosaurus.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1056" data-file-width="2691" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Dynamosaurus.jpg/220px-Dynamosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Dynamosaurus.jpg/330px-Dynamosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Dynamosaurus.jpg/440px-Dynamosaurus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dynamosaurus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_type" title="Biological type">
         Type
        </a>
        specimen of
        <i>
         Dynamosaurus imperiosus
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Teeth from what is now documented as a
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      were found in 1874 by
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Lakes" title="Arthur Lakes">
       Arthur Lakes
      </a>
      near
      <a href="/wiki/Golden,_Colorado" title="Golden, Colorado">
       Golden, Colorado
      </a>
      . In the early 1890s,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Bell_Hatcher" title="John Bell Hatcher">
       John Bell Hatcher
      </a>
      collected postcranial elements in eastern
      <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">
       Wyoming
      </a>
      . The fossils were believed to be from a large species of
      <i>
       Ornithomimus
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       O. grandis
      </i>
      ) but are now considered
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      . Vertebral fragments found by
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_Drinker_Cope" title="Edward Drinker Cope">
       Edward Drinker Cope
      </a>
      in western South Dakota in 1892 and named as
      <i>
       Manospondylus gigas
      </i>
      have also been recognized as belonging to
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quinlanetal2007_160-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-quinlanetal2007-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Barnum_Brown" title="Barnum Brown">
       Barnum Brown
      </a>
      , assistant curator of the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">
       American Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      , found the first partial skeleton of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      in eastern Wyoming in 1900. H. F. Osborn originally named this skeleton
      <i>
       Dynamosaurus imperiosus
      </i>
      in a paper in 1905. Brown found another partial skeleton in the
      <a href="/wiki/Hell_Creek_Formation" title="Hell Creek Formation">
       Hell Creek Formation
      </a>
      in Montana in 1902. Osborn used this
      <a href="/wiki/Holotype" title="Holotype">
       holotype
      </a>
      to describe
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      in the same paper in which
      <i>
       D. imperiosus
      </i>
      was described.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborn1905_67-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-osborn1905-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1906, Osborn recognized the two as synonyms, and acted as first revisor by selecting
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      as the valid name.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborn1906_71-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-osborn1906-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The original
      <i>
       Dynamosaurus
      </i>
      material resides in the collections of the
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">
       Natural History Museum
      </a>
      , London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breithaupt_161-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Breithaupt-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In total, Brown found five
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      partial skeletons. In 1941, Brown's 1902 find was sold to the
      <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Carnegie Museum of Natural History">
       Carnegie Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania. Brown's fourth and largest find, also from Hell Creek, is on display in the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">
       American Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      in New York.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hornerlessem1993_70-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-hornerlessem1993-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
         <span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2010)">
          pageneeded
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Manospondylus">
       <i>
        Manospondylus
       </i>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manospondylus.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="1063" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Manospondylus.jpg/220px-Manospondylus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Manospondylus.jpg/330px-Manospondylus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Manospondylus.jpg/440px-Manospondylus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manospondylus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Illustration of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_type" title="Biological type">
         type specimen
        </a>
        (AMNH 3982) of
        <i>
         Manospondylus gigas
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The first named fossil specimen which can be attributed to
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      consists of two partial vertebrae (one of which has been lost) found by
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_Drinker_Cope" title="Edward Drinker Cope">
       Edward Drinker Cope
      </a>
      in 1892. Cope believed that they belonged to an "agathaumid" (
      <a href="/wiki/Ceratopsidae" title="Ceratopsidae">
       ceratopsid
      </a>
      ) dinosaur, and named them
      <i>
       Manospondylus gigas
      </i>
      , meaning "giant porous vertebra" in reference to the numerous openings for blood vessels he found in the bone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quinlanetal2007_160-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-quinlanetal2007-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <i>
       M. gigas
      </i>
      remains were later identified as those of a theropod rather than a ceratopsid, and H.F. Osborn recognized the similarity between
      <i>
       M. gigas
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      as early as 1917. However, owing to the fragmentary nature of the
      <i>
       Manospondylus
      </i>
      vertebrae, Osborn did not synonymize the two genera.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborn1917_68-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-osborn1917-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In June 2000, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Hills_Institute" title="Black Hills Institute">
       Black Hills Institute
      </a>
      located the type locality of
      <i>
       M. gigas
      </i>
      in South Dakota and unearthed more tyrannosaur bones there. These were judged to represent further remains of the same individual, and to be identical to those of
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manospondyl2000_162-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-manospondyl2000-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the rules of the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Zoological_Nomenclature" title="International Code of Zoological Nomenclature">
       International Code of Zoological Nomenclature
      </a>
      (ICZN), the system that governs the scientific naming of animals,
      <i>
       Manospondylus gigas
      </i>
      should therefore have priority over
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      , because it was named first. However, the Fourth Edition of the ICZN, which took effect on January 1, 2000, states that "the prevailing usage must be maintained" when "the senior synonym or homonym has not been used as a valid name after 1899" and "the junior synonym or homonym has been used for a particular taxon, as its presumed valid name, in at least 25 works, published by at least 10 authors in the immediately preceding 50 years..."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icznart23_163-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-icznart23-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      may qualify as the valid name under these conditions and would most likely be considered a
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomen_protectum" title="Nomen protectum">
        nomen protectum
       </a>
      </i>
      ("protected name") under the ICZN if it is ever formally published on, which it has not yet been.
      <i>
       Manospondylus gigas
      </i>
      could then be deemed a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nomen_oblitum" title="Nomen oblitum">
        nomen oblitum
       </a>
      </i>
      ("forgotten name").
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor2002_164-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-taylor2002-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_specimens">
       Notable specimens
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Specimens_of_Tyrannosaurus" title="Specimens of Tyrannosaurus">
       Specimens of Tyrannosaurus
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sues_skeleton.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2227" data-file-width="3610" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Sues_skeleton.jpg/220px-Sues_skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Sues_skeleton.jpg/330px-Sues_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Sues_skeleton.jpg/440px-Sues_skeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sues_skeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        "Sue" specimen,
        <a href="/wiki/Field_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Field Museum of Natural History">
         Field Museum of Natural History
        </a>
        , Chicago
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Sue_Hendrickson" title="Sue Hendrickson">
       Sue Hendrickson
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Amateur" title="Amateur">
       amateur
      </a>
      paleontologist, discovered the most complete (approximately 85%) and largest
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      fossil skeleton known in the
      <a href="/wiki/Hell_Creek_Formation" title="Hell Creek Formation">
       Hell Creek Formation
      </a>
      near
      <a href="/wiki/Faith,_South_Dakota" title="Faith, South Dakota">
       Faith, South Dakota
      </a>
      , on August 12, 1990. This
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , nicknamed "
      <a href="/wiki/Sue_(dinosaur)" title="Sue (dinosaur)">
       Sue
      </a>
      " in her honor, was the object of a legal battle over its ownership. In 1997 this was settled in favor of Maurice Williams, the original land owner. The fossil collection was purchased by the
      <a href="/wiki/Field_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Field Museum of Natural History">
       Field Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      at auction for USD 7.6 million, making it the most expensive dinosaur skeleton to date. From 1998 to 1999
      <a href="/wiki/Field_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Field Museum of Natural History">
       Field Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      preparators spent over 25,000 man-hours taking the rock off each of the bones.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sueprep_165-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sueprep-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The bones were then shipped off to New Jersey where the mount was made. The finished mount was then taken apart, and along with the bones, shipped back to Chicago for the final assembly. The mounted skeleton opened to the public on May 17, 2000 in the great hall (Stanley Field Hall) at the
      <a href="/wiki/Field_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Field Museum of Natural History">
       Field Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      . A study of this specimen's fossilized bones showed that "Sue" reached full size at age 19 and died at age 28, the longest any tyrannosaur is known to have lived.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ericksonetal2004TyrannosaurGigantism_166-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ericksonetal2004TyrannosaurGigantism-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Early speculation that Sue may have died from a bite to the back of the head was not confirmed. Though subsequent study showed many pathologies in the skeleton, no bite marks were found.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brochu2003_167-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brochu2003-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Damage to the back of the skull may have been caused by post-mortem trampling. Recent speculation indicates that "Sue" may have died of starvation after contracting a parasitic infection from eating diseased meat; the resulting infection would have caused inflammation in the throat, ultimately leading "Sue" to starve because she could no longer swallow food. This hypothesis is substantiated by smooth-edged holes in her skull which are similar to those caused in modern-day birds that contract the same parasite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DSC02558_Bucky_(T.rex_fossil),_Kelsey_(Triceratops_fossil)_and_Stan_(T._rex_cast).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2082" data-file-width="3456" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DSC02558_Bucky_%28T.rex_fossil%29%2C_Kelsey_%28Triceratops_fossil%29_and_Stan_%28T._rex_cast%29.jpg/220px-DSC02558_Bucky_%28T.rex_fossil%29%2C_Kelsey_%28Triceratops_fossil%29_and_Stan_%28T._rex_cast%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DSC02558_Bucky_%28T.rex_fossil%29%2C_Kelsey_%28Triceratops_fossil%29_and_Stan_%28T._rex_cast%29.jpg/330px-DSC02558_Bucky_%28T.rex_fossil%29%2C_Kelsey_%28Triceratops_fossil%29_and_Stan_%28T._rex_cast%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DSC02558_Bucky_%28T.rex_fossil%29%2C_Kelsey_%28Triceratops_fossil%29_and_Stan_%28T._rex_cast%29.jpg/440px-DSC02558_Bucky_%28T.rex_fossil%29%2C_Kelsey_%28Triceratops_fossil%29_and_Stan_%28T._rex_cast%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DSC02558_Bucky_(T.rex_fossil),_Kelsey_(Triceratops_fossil)_and_Stan_(T._rex_cast).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Skeleton of "Bucky" and cast of "Stan", at the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Children%27s_Museum_of_Indianapolis" title="Children's Museum of Indianapolis">
         Children's Museum of Indianapolis
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Another
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      , nicknamed "Stan", in honor of amateur paleontologist Stan Sacrison, was found in the Hell Creek Formation near
      <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_South_Dakota" title="Buffalo, South Dakota">
       Buffalo, South Dakota
      </a>
      , in the spring of 1987. It was not collected until 1992, as it was mistakenly thought to be a
      <i>
       Triceratops
      </i>
      skeleton. Stan is 63% complete and is on display in the Black Hills Institute of Geological Research in
      <a href="/wiki/Hill_City,_South_Dakota" title="Hill City, South Dakota">
       Hill City, South Dakota
      </a>
      , after an extensive world tour during 1995 and 1996.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2008_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-larson2008-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This tyrannosaur, too, was found to have many bone pathologies, including broken and healed ribs, a broken (and healed) neck and a spectacular hole in the back of its head, about the size of a
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      tooth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurusscale.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="872" data-file-width="2543" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Tyrannosaurusscale.png/220px-Tyrannosaurusscale.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Tyrannosaurusscale.png/330px-Tyrannosaurusscale.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Tyrannosaurusscale.png/440px-Tyrannosaurusscale.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurusscale.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Size of various specimens compared with a human
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the summer of 2000, Jack Horner discovered five
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      skeletons near the Fort Peck Reservoir in Montana. One of the specimens was reported to be perhaps the largest
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      ever found.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-horner_170-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bbc-horner-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2001, a 50% complete skeleton of a juvenile
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      was discovered in the Hell Creek Formation in Montana, by a crew from the
      <a href="/wiki/Burpee_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Burpee Museum of Natural History">
       Burpee Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      of Rockford, Illinois. Dubbed "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jane_(dinosaur)" title="Jane (dinosaur)">
       Jane
      </a>
      ", the find was initially considered the first known skeleton of the pygmy tyrannosaurid
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nanotyrannus" title="Nanotyrannus">
        Nanotyrannus
       </a>
      </i>
      but subsequent research has revealed that it is more likely a juvenile
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
       <a href="#cite_note-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is the most complete and best preserved juvenile example known to date. Jane has been examined by
      <a href="/wiki/Jack_Horner_(paleontologist)" title="Jack Horner (paleontologist)">
       Jack Horner
      </a>
      , Pete Larson,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Bakker" title="Robert Bakker">
       Robert Bakker
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregory_M_Erickson" title="Gregory M Erickson">
       Greg Erickson
      </a>
      , and several other renowned
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleontologist" title="Paleontologist">
       paleontologists
      </a>
      , because of the uniqueness of her age. "Jane" is currently on exhibit at the Burpee Museum of Natural History in Rockford, Illinois.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
       <a href="#cite_note-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visitjane_173-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-visitjane-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In a press release on April 7, 2006,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bozeman_Campus,_Montana_State_University,_USA" title="Bozeman Campus, Montana State University, USA">
       Bozeman Campus, Montana State University, USA
      </a>
      revealed that it possessed the largest
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus
      </i>
      skull yet discovered. Discovered in the 1960s and only recently reconstructed, the skull measures 59 inches (150cm) long compared to the 55.4 inches (141cm) of "Sue's" skull, a difference of 6.5%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">
       In popular culture
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus_in_popular_culture" title="Tyrannosaurus in popular culture">
       Tyrannosaurus in popular culture
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Since it was first described in 1905,
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      has become the most widely recognized dinosaur species in
      <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">
       popular culture
      </a>
      . It is the only dinosaur that is commonly known to the general public by its full scientific name (
      <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">
       binomial name
      </a>
      ) (
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      ), and the scientific abbreviation
      <i>
       T.rex
      </i>
      has also come into wide usage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brochu2003_21-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-brochu2003-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._Bakker" title="Robert T. Bakker">
       Robert T. Bakker
      </a>
      notes this in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Dinosaur_Heresies" title="The Dinosaur Heresies">
        The Dinosaur Heresies
       </a>
      </i>
      and explains that a name like "
      <i>
       Tyrannosaurus rex
      </i>
      is just irresistible to the tongue."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1986_13-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-bakker1986-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation journal">
          Erickson, Gregory M.; Makovicky, Peter J.;
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phil_Currie" title="Phil Currie">
           Currie, Philip J.
          </a>
          ; Norell, Mark A.; Yerby, Scott A.; Brochu, Christopher A. (2004). "Gigantism and comparative life-history parameters of tyrannosaurid dinosaurs".
          <i>
           Nature
          </i>

       <li id="cite_note-Breithaupt-161">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-Breithaupt_161-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation journal">
          Breithaupt, Brent H.; Southwell, Elizabeth H.; Matthews, Neffra A. (2006). Lucas, S. G.; Sullivan, R. M., eds.
          <a class="external text" href="http://econtent.unm.edu/utils/getdownloaditem/collection/bulletins/id/693/filename/694.pdf/mapsto/pdf" rel="nofollow">
           "
           <i>
            Dynamosaurus imperiosus
           </i>
           and the earliest discoveries of
           <i>
            Tyrannosaurus rex
           </i>
           in Wyoming and the West"
          </a>
          <span style="font-size:85%;">
           (PDF)
          </span>
          .
          <i>
           New Mexico Museum of Natural History and Science Bulletin
          </i>
          <b>
           35
          </b>
          : 258.
          <q>
           The original skeleton of
           <i>
            Dynamosaurus imperiosus
           </i>
           (AMNH 5866/ BM R7995), together with other
           <i>
            T. rex
           </i>
           material (including parts of AMNH 973, 5027, and 5881), were sold to the British Museum of Natural History (now The Natural History Museum) in 1960. This material was used in an interesting 'half-mount' display of this dinosaur in London. Currently the material resides in the research collections.
          </q>
         </span>
